    Historical Records - Miscellaneous Records - External Compilation

    Records

    "Chronicles of Emperors": Fuxi observed the patterns in the sky and the laws on the earth, studied the markings of birds and beasts along with the suitability of the land, drew from the human body and objects around him. Thus, he created writing characters to replace the primitive practice of knotting ropes for communication, and devised the Eight Trigrams to convey the virtues of the divine and to categorize the emotions of all things. This led to the understanding of the six energies and six cavities, the five organs and five elements, the yin and yang of the four seasons, the rise and fall of water and fire, resulting in the manifestation of images. The principles of all illnesses could then be inferred, and he analyzed a hundred herbs to develop nine needles for the rescue of the ailing.[1p]
    In "The History of Roads": Fuxi observed the six qi, examined yin and yang, and applied them to his own body. Thus, the rise and fall of fire and water through the four seasons were given forms, and the principles behind various illnesses were categorized. He then attempted to create treatments using needles to address the ailments of the people, leading them to greater trust. [Note: In ancient times, needles were used, but later generations used a different method. The mountains of Gao have many such needles.]

    Supplement from "Records of the Grand Historian": Shennong used a red whip to hit plants, beginning to taste all herbs and thus establishing medicine. From "The Chronicles of Emperors": The Flame Emperor, Shennong, grew by the rivers, teaching the world to cultivate grains for food, saving lives and reducing killings. He tasted herbs to declare their medicinal properties, saving lives from misfortunes, which people used daily without knowing. He authored the "Compendium of Materia Medica" in four volumes.

    From "Records of the Search for Spirits": Shennong wielded a red whip on countless herbs, comprehending their properties of being balanced, poisonous, cold, or warm, and their scents and flavors. He disseminated all grains and thus was honored as the Divine Farmer Emperor.

    From "External Records": In ancient times, when the people fell ill and were ignorant of remedies, the Flame Emperor began to taste herbs and plants, observed their cold, warm, and balanced properties, and discerned the roles of chief, minister, assistant, and guide. In one day, he encountered seventy poisons and transformed them miraculously. Consequently, he authored texts to treat the maladies of the people, marking the beginning of medicine. He further assessed the sweetness and bitterness of springs, guiding the people on what to avoid and seek. As a result, the populace lived in peace and had strong livelihoods, free from premature death, which was beneficial to all under heaven.[2p]
    In the "History of the Road," Shennong asked the Little Boy of Taiyi: "In ancient times, people lived beyond a hundred years, but later generations do not investigate the years determined by heaven, resulting in the blame of untimely death. What breath or essence accounts for this?" The Little Boy replied: "Heaven has nine gates, with the middle path being the best. Thus, he studied the Great Beginning, examined the jade book, ground the shellfish whip, analyzed colors and odors, and tasted herbs and plants to correct their names. He scrutinized their toxicity, marked their dryness and coldness, observed their fears and aversions, discerned their ministerial uses, clarified and corrected them to nourish life and treat illness. In one day, he dealt with seventy poisons, reaching to the essence of breath. There were four hundred diseases recorded, with three hundred sixty-five medicines noted in the 'Herbal Classics,' exceeding which would lead to chaos. He commissioned loans for seasonal management of color and pulse, observed harmony and alignment, applied techniques to benefit the world, and people could maintain their lives."

    [Note: Ren Shuyi mentions that in Taiyuan, there is a divine cauldron hill and a cauldron used by Shennong to test medicines. Additionally, within Chengyang Mountain, there is a place where Shennong whips medicinal herbs. Shennong is originally known for his connection to herbs, and there is a Ziyang Temple in the mountain where the emperor distinguished medicines.]

    In the "Comprehensive Records," in the Annals of the Three Sovereigns: At the time of the Flame Emperor Shennong, he experimented with a hundred herbs daily, experiencing life and death a hundred times. He obtained three hundred sixty items to correspond with the number of the celestial cycle, which later generations recorded as a book, calling it the "Herbal Classics of Shennong." He also composed formulas to treat contemporary diseases.

    In the "Chronicles of Emperors": The Yellow Emperor, of the Xiong clan, commanded Lei Gong and Qi Bo to discuss the meridians, addressing eighty-one inquiries for the "Classic of Difficulties." He taught the use of nine needles, compiling the "Classics of Internal and External Techniques" in eighteen volumes.[3p]
    In the "History of the Roads": The Yellow Emperor, of the Bear clan, said: In the life of man, one carries the yin and embraces the yang, consumes flavors and is adorned with colors, swayed by cold and heat from outside, while joy and anger surge from within. Misfortunes and early death alternate between rulers and subjects. Hence, one should explore the extremes above and below, examine the five energies, establish the five movements, penetrate the essence of life, record the yin and yang, consulting deeply the teachings of Qi and Lei to create the "Inner Canon." Careful observation of the timing leads to inscribing it on jade tablets to be stored in the chamber of the spirit of orchids.

    He explored grains in the granary and commanded Yu to examine the hall of clarity with Bai Bo to investigate the pulse, observing the timing diligently, ensuring comprehensive safety. He ordered Wu to prepare a prescription for medicinal elixirs, overseeing purification and treatment, allowing people to live to their full years.

    (Note in "Dao Ji Jing": Grains refer to the sage of grains, whose practice continues without rest for longevity. Wang Mang usurped the throne, planting five-colored crops in the palace, positioning them according to color, claiming this was the technique of the Yellow Emperor’s sage of grains. "Ling Shu" also discusses this. The "Yellow Emperor's Original Genesis Canon" mentions: The blood avoids the timing of yin and yang's vital energies, in the heavenly central positions, also known as the heavenly thieves, particularly avoiding needle moxibustion. "Su Wen": By diligently observing the timing, the energies align, allowing combinations of color and pulse, leading to comprehensive safety. There are fifty-nine needling points below the stomach for fevers, detailed in "Su Wen". The Yellow Emperor further inquired about treating ailments, where he commanded Qi Bo to taste a hundred herbs, overseeing medical practice; thus, the canon and herbal texts all originate from this. Therefore, "Jia Yu" states: The Yellow Emperor tasted the herbs and plants.)[4p]
    "Tongzhi" Record of the Three Sovereigns: The Yellow Emperor of the Xuanyuan clan observed the five movements and six energies, and thus composed the questions to Qibo, which is known as the "Inner Canon." Some say that the "Inner Canon" was written by later generations, but is based on the Yellow Emperor.

    "Zuo Zhuan": In the twenty-first year of Duke Xiang's reign, during summer, the ruler of Chu sent Fenzi to be the chief administrator, consulting with Shen Shuyu. Shuyu said: "If a nation is filled with favor and the king is weak, then the nation cannot be sustained." Thus, he excused himself due to illness. As the summer heat intensified, ice appeared beneath the bed. Wearing dense silk robes, he ate little and slept. The ruler of Chu sent a doctor to examine him, who reported: "He is extremely emaciated, but his blood and qi have not stirred." He then appointed Zi Nan as the chief administrator.

    "Guo Yu": At the meeting of Wen, the Jin people captured Duke Cheng and returned him to Zhou, where he was poisoned by a doctor. Since he did not die, the doctor was not executed. [Note: Duke Cheng relied on Chu and did not serve Jin, and he also killed his younger brother, Shuwu. His minister, Yuan Zhi, reported this to Jin, leading to Wen Gong capturing him. The poison bird is toxic; drinking its venom leads to instant death. It would be better to pay the doctor to dilute the poison, allowing him to survive. The doctor is not punished, for it conceals private malice in administering poison.][5p]
    In the "Muwang" chapter of Liezi, it is said that a man named Huazi from Song suffered from a chronic illness causing him to forget. He would forget in the morning what he learned in the evening, forget what he had in the evening by morning, forget how to walk on the road, and forget how to sit in his house. Now, he did not recognize the past, nor did he recognize the present. His entire household was distressed by this. He consulted historians and tried divination, but it yielded no answers; he sought the help of shamans and prayed, but was met with no respite; he approached doctors and sought treatments, but it was futile.

    In Lu, there was a Confucian scholar who claimed he could cure him. Huazi's wife and children asked him to use half of their estate in exchange for a remedy. The scholar said: "This is not something that can be determined by divination, nor through prayer, nor by medicine. I will try to transform his heart and change his thoughts, hoping for a cure." Thus, he experimented by exposing Huazi to light when he sought clothes, providing food when he was hungry, and seeking brightness when he was in darkness.

    The scholar joyfully told Huazi's children, "The illness can be cured." However, I will not inform anyone of my secret method; let us keep it private for seven days. Following this, no one knew what he did. After many years of illness, on one morning, it all vanished. Once Huazi regained his senses, he became extremely angry, dismissed his wife, punished his children, and chased away the scholar. The people of Song caught him and questioned him. Huazi said: "I used to forget, obliviously unaware of the existence or non-existence of heaven and earth. Now I have suddenly grasped the past and can remember losses and gains from decades, the sorrows and joys, all the tangled emotions arise within me. I fear that in the future there will be turmoil from losses and gains, sorrows and joys—my heart is like this. Can the momentary forgetfulness be regained?"

    Zi Gong heard this and was amazed, reporting it to Confucius. Confucius said, "This is beyond your understanding, isn't it?" He then instructed Yan Hui to remember it.[6p]
    "The Disciple Gongsun: Confucius was unwell, and Duke Ai sent a physician to examine him. The physician asked, 'How is your living situation and diet?' Confucius replied, 'In spring, I stay in a thatched hut; in summer, I live in Miyan; in autumn, there is no wind, and in winter, I do not suffer from heat. My food is not lacking, and I do not indulge in drinking.' The physician said, 'This is a good doctor indeed.'"[7p]
    "Records of the Warring States": Duke Ling of Wei had close ties with the eunuch Mi Zi Xia, both of whom held the power to influence the ruler. A spy named Tu Zhen reported to the duke, saying: "In the past, I dreamed of you." The duke asked: "What kind of dream?" The reply was: "I dreamed of the Kitchen God." The duke became angry and said: "I have heard that dreaming of a ruler is like dreaming of the sun. If you say you dreamed of the Kitchen God, that could be explained, but without explanation, you die." The reply was: "The sun illuminates the world; nothing can obscure it. But the kitchen is different; if those before block the view, those after cannot see at all. Thus, I doubt there is someone obstructing you." The duke said: "Good." Consequently, he dismissed Mi Zi Xia and appointed Si Kong Xun.

    "He Guan Zi": The three brothers of Bian Que were all skilled in medicine. Duke Wen of Wei asked: "Which of the three brothers is the best?" The reply was: "The eldest brother observes the color and thus does not leave home; the second brother observes the tiniest details and also does not leave the door; Bian Que uses needles to pierce blood vessels and administers poisons, earning fame among the lords."

    "Spring and Autumn of Wu and Yue": Year fifteen of Gou Jian's plan to attack Wu, he mourned the dead and inquired about the wounded, expressed sorrow for those who lament, and joy for those who celebrate. He called his father, brothers, and siblings, swearing to them: "If the generals are to be spared, let it be reported to me; let the physicians take care of them."[8p]
    In the "Strategies of the Warring States": When Jing Ke arrived at Qin, he presented a map of the region of Duhang. As the map was unfolded, his dagger appeared. He then grasped the sleeve of the Qin king with his left hand and held the dagger with his right hand, preparing to strike. Before he could approach, the Qin king was startled and stood up, causing the sleeve to tear; he drew his sword, which was too long for him to wield properly in his chamber. In his panic, the sword was stuck and couldn't be drawn easily. Jing Ke pursued the Qin king, who ran around a column. The ministers were astonished, caught off guard, and completely lost their composure. According to Qin law, the ministers who attended the throne were not permitted to carry weapons; those in the inner court held weapons, but they were positioned below the throne and could not act without a decree. In that urgent moment, there was no time to summon soldiers, which allowed Jing Ke to pursue the Qin king without being attacked effectively; instead, they attempted to grab him with their hands. At that time, the physician Xia Wuqie used his medicine pouch to assist Jing Ke. The Qin king was running around the column in a state of panic and didn’t know what to do. Those around him shouted: "The king is unarmed! The king is unarmed!" He then drew his sword and struck Jing Ke, injuring his left thigh. Jing Ke was incapacitated and tried to use his dagger to strike the Qin king, but missed and hit the column instead. The Qin king retaliated, inflicting eight wounds upon Jing Ke. Realizing that his plan had failed, Jing Ke leaned against the column, laughed, and scornfully said: "The reason this mission did not succeed is that I aimed to force a situation and had to agree to a contract in order to reward the crown prince." As those around him advanced, they executed Jing Ke. The Qin king, feeling dizzy for a long time, eventually awarded titles and rewards to the ministers and those present, with each receiving varying honors, and granted Xia Wuqie two hundred taels of gold, saying: "Wuqie loves me, for he used his medicine pouch to assist Jing Ke."[9p]
    In the Records of the Grand Historian, there is a passage about Emperor Gao. When he was faced with a calamity, he was struck by a stray arrow and fell ill while traveling. His illness was severe, and Empress Lu welcomed a skilled physician. When the physician entered to see him, Gao asked about his condition. The physician replied, "The illness can be treated." Emperor Gao then scornfully berated him, saying, "I, a commoner, took up a three-foot sword to seize the realm; is this not Heaven's decree? Life and death are in the hands of Heaven; what good is even Bian Que?" He refused to allow treatment and dismissed the physician, bestowing him with fifty pounds of gold.[10p]
    Kuli Lie Zhuan: Yi Zong, a man from Hedong, in his youth engaged in banditry with Zhang Cgong. Zong had a sister, who, through her medical skills, gained favor with the Empress Dowager. The Empress Dowager asked, "Do you have any sons or brothers in official positions?" The sister replied, "I have a brother who is unworthy, he cannot serve." The Empress Dowager then informed the emperor, and appointed Yi Zong’s brother Zong as a mid-level official, assigning him to the position of magistrate of Shangdang County.

    Wanshi Zhang Shu Lie Zhuan: The Langzhong Ling, Zhou Wen, whose name was Ren, originally from Rencheng, was recognized for his medical skills. When Emperor Jing was crown prince, he appointed him as a gentleman attendant.

    Han Shu Xu Huanghou Zhuan: Lady Huo, desiring to elevate her youngest daughter, found no way. The following year, when Empress Xu fell ill during her pregnancy, the court physician Chunyu Yan, favored by the Huo family, entered the palace to attend to the empress's illness. Yan was rewarded and appointed as a palace guard, asked to facilitate a meeting with Lady Huo on behalf of the empress. Yan reported this to Xian. Xian then harbored ulterior motives and addressed Yan, saying, "If you could favor me in this matter, I too wish to repay you, is that acceptable?" Yan replied, "What you say is entirely appropriate." Xian continued, "The general has always favored my youngest daughter, wanting to elevate her through marriage, I wish to entrust this matter to you." Yan asked, "What do you mean?" Xian explained, "Once a woman is past nursing, it is a matter of life and death. Now the empress is due to give birth, it would be possible to poison her and have my daughter become the empress instead."[11p]
    If we are fortunate to succeed, wealth and honor will be shared with the young lord. Yan said: For medical treatment, one must first taste; how could that be? Xian replied: It is for the young lord to decide. The general commands the world; who would dare to speak? It is a matter of urgency and caution, fearing that the young lord may not be interested. After a long time, Yan said: I wish to do my utmost. He ground the aconite and brought it into the Longding Palace. After the queen's dismissal, Yan took the aconite and mixed it with the great medicine and large pills to administer to the queen. After a while, she said: My head is dizzy; is there no poison in the medicine? He replied: There is none. However, she became increasingly distressed and collapsed. Yan came out and saw Xian; they inquired about each other but did not dare to express gratitude to Yan heavily. Later, there were reports accusing physicians who attended the sick without propriety, all were arrested and sent to the Zhao prison for admonition. Xian, fearing the urgency of the situation, mentioned this to Guang. He then said: Since the plan has failed, do not let officials pressure Yan. Guang was startled and remained silent. Afterward, a report concluded that Yan should not be questioned.

    From the "Miscellaneous Records of the Western Capital": The wife of Huo Guang bequeathed to Yan twenty-four pieces of purple peach brocade and twenty-five pieces of scatter flower silk. The silk came from the family of Chen Baoguang of Julu. The wife of Baoguang passed on the technique; Huo Xian summoned them to make it, using one hundred and twenty tweezers, taking sixty days to produce one piece, each piece worth ten thousand coins. Furthermore, he provided one gem-engraving instrument, one hundred pieces of green silk, one million coins, and one hundred taels of gold to establish a residence, with countless servants. Yan still complained: What good have I done for you to repay me like this?[12p]
    "Zhongli Yi's Biography": Huang Dang was the governor of Kuaiji. In the fourteenth year of Jianwu, a severe plague struck Wu, and he was appointed as the district officer to oversee postal services. He then concealed himself, without a cap, and personally visited the homes of the infected, providing them with medicine. He prayed and offered sacrifices at the temples for the people, summoning a hundred physicians to combine herbal remedies. Fearing that young or incompetent doctors might harm the people with toxic doses, he first tested the medicines on himself before administering them. He cared for over four thousand patients, all of whom recovered. Later, when the governor went out, the people, suffering from disasters, clung to his carriage in tears, saying: "There is no need for the governor to come out; if we have Officer Zhong, all the people will be saved."

    "Tongzhi" concerning Xu Yang: Yang, styled Weijun, was from Pingyu in Runan and had an early interest in numerology. When Wang Mang was in power, he summoned Yang as an attendant and gradually promoted him to command the army at Jiuquan. When Mang usurped the throne, Yang changed his name to become a wizard and physician, fleeing to another region. When Mang was defeated, he returned to his hometown.

    "Eastern View of Han History": Deng Xun was humble and forgiving, treating subordinates without regard for status, welcoming friends as if they were his own children, and correcting faults with encouragement. The court physician Pi Xun, returning from a hunt in the Upper Forest, stayed overnight at the palace gate and suffered from a cold-related illness. At that time, Xun was attending to his duties when he heard Xun's distress. He got up to inquire. Xun said: "I wish to have fire to warm my back." Xun went to the main palace gate seeking fire but could not find any; he then breathed on Xun's back with his mouth. He called others of the same group to join him in this effort, and by morning he had fully recovered.[13p]
    "Records of the Lost": Sun He cherished Lady Deng, and often kept her on his lap. Under the moonlight, as he danced with a water jade ruyi, he accidentally injured her cheek, causing blood to stain her clothes, leaving her in great distress. He licked her wound and commanded the court physician to prepare medicine. The physician said, "If you acquire the marrow of a white otter, mixed with jade and amber dust, it will eradicate this mark." He spent a hundred gold pieces to obtain these, and thus ordered the salve to be made. The physician mistakenly included too much amber, resulting in red spots like vermilion. Upon closer inspection, it only enhanced her beauty.

    "Book of the Rise of Jin": Cheng Ju served as the chief imperial physician. When Emperor Wu initially received Wei Shen, he changed the era name to Taishi, and Ju presented a pheasant-feather cloak. The emperor, seeing it as an unusual craft and attire forbidden by court etiquette, burned it in front of the palace. Cheng, grateful for his medical skills, was allowed to enter and exit the restricted areas. Because of Lady Jia, he prepared a combination of ba dou (kusha) and apricot kernel pills, which harmed the sorrowful Crown Prince, leading to his execution.

    "Essentials of the Yuan": In the winter of the ninth year of Yuan Kang, on the day of Renxu, Jia Shuren killed the Crown Prince. Xu Chuan states the Crown Prince's name was Yu, and initially, Lady Jia ordered the physician Cheng Ju to prepare the ba dou pills, causing the Crown Prince to take them and die.

    "Book of Jin": The biography of King Jian of Qi, Min Wang Ji: Ji, styled Jingzhi, was the son of King You. He was known for his kindness and generosity, embodying his father's virtues. When his father fell ill, Emperor Wu did not believe it, sending physicians for diagnosis, all of whom reported no illness. Upon his father's death, Ji lamented, alleging that his father had been deceived by the physicians, leading to their immediate execution by imperial decree.[14p]
    Yan Han Zhuan: Brother Han fell ill in Jiangning and sought treatment on his own, but died at the physician's home. His family, upon mourning, found that the mourning banner was entangled around a tree and could not be untied. The mourners stumbled and fell. He, while still speaking, said: "I have not yet fulfilled my lifespan; it was just too much medicine that harmed my internal organs. I am about to be revived, so please do not bury me." His parents complied, and they opened the coffin. Indeed, he showed signs of life; he scraped the coffin with his hand, and his fingers were all injured, yet his breath was very faint, and life and death were indistinguishable. Nourishment was provided, but even after several months, he could not speak. What he needed for food and drink, he entrusted to dreams. After thirteen years, he ultimately did not rise.

    Wang Wei Zhuan in the Book of Song: Wei, styled Jingyuan, was a native of Lingyi in Langya. In his youth, he loved learning and understood everything thoroughly. His younger brother Sengqian fell ill, and Wei personally attended to his treatment. However, Sengqian took medicine in excess and ultimately passed away. Wei felt deep self-blame and fell ill himself, unable to treat again. Grieving for Sengqian, he could not be consoled and wrote to the spirit: "I was just wanting to cultivate the forest and marsh to extend my years. What sin have I committed? Seeing this untimely death by my hand brings me pain and hatred. I have always loved medicine, yet I did not allow my disciple to be whole; furthermore, I did not think thoroughly enough, resulting in wrongful errors. Thinking of this one matter brings me particular grief. What can I do about this pain? What about my guilt?" Four weeks after Sengqian's death, Wei also passed away.[15p]
    Liu Huaishen's Biography: The emperor favored Concubine Yin, who passed away. After her burial, he often mourned at her grave along with the officials; he also had the physician Yang Zhi cry for the Yin family, and Zhi wept as well. On another day, someone asked, “How did you come to have such urgent tears, Zhi?” Zhi, who had recently lost his beloved concubine, replied, “I was weeping for my deceased wife.” Zhi was known for his humor and ability to make jokes, which the emperor enjoyed.

    The Book of Liang, Ancestral Records: Ancestral Zuo, named Yi, was the seventh son of Gao Ancestral. At birth, he suffered from an eye condition; Gao Ancestral intended to treat it, but as a result, he became blind in one eye.

    The Book of Wei, Transmission of the Western Regions: In the country of Yuebian, the True Lord sent envoys for nine years to offer tributes and also sent sorcerers, claiming they could cut people's throats causing blood to flow, and strike a person's head causing the bones to cave in, resulting in a loss of blood, whether several liters or a bucket full. They would then use herbal medicine to fill the person's mouth, instructing them to chew and swallow it, and soon the blood would stop, with wounds healing in a month without scars. Ancestral Zuo, doubting its authenticity, had death row inmates test it, and it was confirmed. They claimed that such herbs existed on all of China’s famous mountains, leading him to bring in practitioners of the technique and treat them well.

    The Record of Uncommon Stories: At the end of the Sui dynasty, Gao Kaidao was shot with an arrow that penetrated his bone, and when he ordered a physician to remove it, the physician was unable to do so. Kaidao asked him, who replied, “I fear the king's pain.” Kaidao then executed him and summoned another physician, who claimed, “I can remove it.” He used a small axe to press down on the wound's edge, drove a small stick two inches into the bone, and pulled it out with a pair of pliers. Kaidao remained calm while drinking and eating. He awarded the physician with three hundred bolts of silk, but later, he was killed by General Zhang Jinshu.[16p]
    "In the 'Record of Conversations': Han the Assistant Minister Gao was afflicted with a small sore, and he asked the doctor to apply an ointment, but the medicine did not absorb. When the Duke inquired, the doctor replied, 'It is cold, and the ointment is hard.'

    In the 'Casual Talks of the Jade Hall': During the prosperous days in Chang'an, there was a family selling herbal medicines in the western market. They used only a few common ingredients, did not inquire about illnesses, and simply charged a hundred wen for one dose, which could cure a thousand kinds of ailments upon consumption. They usually had a large pot in a spacious home, grinding and cooking day and night, unable to keep up with the demand. People came from near and far to purchase it, and the market was bustling, as even during the months of summer, there were those who had not yet received their remedies. They profited greatly.

    At that time, there was a patient named Tian Lingzi, and he had summoned every doctor in the land, even the royal physician under summons, but no one could ascertain his ailment. Suddenly, a friend advised Tian, saying, 'Why not try the herbal medicine from the western market?' Lingzi replied, 'Indeed.' He then sent a servant promptly to fetch it. The servant secured the medicine and hurried back, but as he approached the neighborhood, his horse stumbled and fell. The servant, fearing the master’s wrath and unable to return for more, went to a dye shop and begged for a bottle from the dye vat, deceiving the owner. After consuming it, Lingzi's illness was immediately cured. Tian only knew that he was healed and did not understand where the remedy had come from, thus he compensated the herbalist handsomely, and the reputation of the herbal medicine family rose. There had also been a physician named Zhang from Ye in recent years who, similarly, accumulated a vast stock and became famous, eventually taken back by the foreign king."[17p]
    "Records of Imperial Court Submissions": Bi Yan, the son of a merchant, advanced through the ranks in the civil service examinations. Du Cong served as the military governor of Huainan and set up his tent there. He initially regulated the salt trade and was well-versed in literature, handling matters without delay. At the Hanlin Academy, he received exceptional favor from the emperor and was allowed to be considered for the prime minister role, which made him deeply resented by the prime minister Linghu Tao. After moving from Binning to Fengtian, Zhao Yi, and the North Gate, all these actions were to slow down Tao’s plans for the prime minister position. Yan attempted to forge a bond with Tao by acquiring a rare beauty at the North Gate, someone not easily found in the mortal world. Decorated in fine clothing, she was sent to be presented to Tao. Upon seeing her, Tao’s heart was stirred and he told his son, "Such a beauty will definitely bring harm." Yan was not favored by the Taiyuan family; now with this bait, they aim to benefit the clan. After just one meeting, she was sent away. A special envoy was afraid to return, waiting for Yan's instructions. Yan then poured out his blood in a letter to Tao, but Tao ultimately rejected the proposal and ordered the local officials to trade her.

    At the eastern pavilion, Dr. Li Yuanbo, favored by the emperor, was entrusted with seventy thousand cash for his home and then made to serve in the main hall. Within a month, he had won the favor of the emperor, and he was subsequently presented to the emperor. Upon seeing him, the emperor was enchanted, and Li's favor surpassed that of all the palace's residents. Yuanbo offered the emperor a concoction of cinnabar to gain favor, which led to the emperor suffering from an illness, all of which was Li Yuanbo's fault. When Emperor Yi Zong ascended the throne, Li Yuanbo, alongside the hermit Wang Yue and the Taoist Yu Zizhi, were executed in the marketplace.[18p]
    "Book of the Banana": During the Tang dynasty, Li Bozhen received a medical prescription stating: "One piece of white gold is prepared for the need of orange-yellow." Initially, I did not understand its meaning, but upon reading "Continued Sayings of the World," I found: "The loquat is yellow, and the doctor is busy; the orange is yellow, and the doctor hides." So I realized it was due to the time.

    "Supplement to National History": Bai Cen once met an unusual person who shared a formula with him, which proved to be fully effective. Cen showcased it for profit but later became a minor general in Huainan. The governor Gao Shi forcibly took his formula, but it did not work very well. Cen eventually went to Jiujiang, where he was eaten by a tiger. The courier found the true prescription in his bag and delivered it, writing it down for dissemination.

    "Records of Wild Travels": During the Five Dynasties, Zhu Jin served Yang Xingmi and once suffered from a sore. The doctor was fearful upon examining him. Jin said: "Just treat it; I am not one who dies from illness." When Xu Wen and his son seized power, Jin plotted to eliminate them but was killed instead and buried at the north gate of Guangling. At that time, many people were afflicted by malaria, taking soil from Jin's grave and mixing it with water to drink, which would instantly cure them. He knew he would not die from illness in life, yet after death, the soil from his grave could still heal others; indeed, he was a remarkable person.[19p]
    From "History of Liao," Biography of Yelü Shucheng: Initially, the Khitan doctors were not well-versed in pulse diagnosis and medicinal prescriptions. The emperor ordered Shucheng to translate books on pulse diagnosis for wide circulation. From this point on, everyone became proficient, and even various clans understood medical practices.

    From "History of Song," Biography of the Second Daughter of Emperor Xiaozong: The Princess of Changjia was granted the title of Shuo Ren in the twenty-fourth year of Shaoxing and promoted to the title of Yongjia District Princess. She passed away in the thirty-second year. The edict designated the medical officials, such as Li Shike and others, to her attendants. During Xiaozong's time in the Eastern Palace, he reported that his young daughter was frequently ill and that the physicians should not be blamed, thus the matter was put to rest.

    From "Records of Talks by the Yan in Mianshui": During the reign of Xiangfu, some princes, due to illness, sought treatment from doctors with success and requested exemptions from far-off districts. Emperor Zhenzong remarked that having a physician attend to a district was not a matter of good governance but could be rewarded generously. He also instructed the chancellor to convey this intention.

    From "Talks Garden": There is a saying in the capital: "When a death is pronounced by the physician, the edict for burial can ruin a family." This stems from the sent physicians who say, "I have come under imperial decree; the prescription must be submitted with adjustments in order," often forcing patients to take medication until death ensues. For families under such edicts, they are required to wash their hands and wear specific cloths; three pieces each in white for individuals, with other items being customary. During the Yuanyou period, Duke Han Kang suffered from an illness, and upon the physician's observation, he was given a golden liquid pill. Although he could eat and drink for a while, the old duke's vital energy was diminished, unable to withstand external factors, leading to his death. The court dispatched envoys to inquire about subsequent matters, and due to confusion, an edict for burial was mistakenly promised, which later led to his sons and nephews distancing themselves from it.[20p]
    Teng Yuanfa said: A good physician advises to use the white character herbs from the Materia Medica, which have been proven effective. Su Zirong remarked that the black characters are later additions by others.

    In "Tan Pu": Xu Junping, a native of Jinling, personally witnessed Jing Gong's illness change. He alone slept in the same bed as a physician. Jing Gong suddenly awoke and said: "Just now I dreamed of standing together with Wang Yuyu, both without headgear, on the same platform." Shortly thereafter, he passed away, which is quite strange.

    In "Zhou Mie Lu": Wang Wengong from the east of the state had a serious illness. The True Temple repeatedly sent physicians to check on him, but they could not find a solution, so they returned after six months like this. One day, the Wang family announced his death, and the physicians said to others: "For half a year, there was an unbreakable bond with a medicine, yet everyone is now separated."

    In "Mengxi Bitan": Li Yuqing, a professor in the National Academy, was knowledgeable about politics and firm in discarding evils. Malefactors and corrupt officials feared him like a deity. In his later years, he suffered from a severe illness. There were many local physicians known for their malice, and they often feared exposure by Yuqing. Because of his condition, they administered toxic medicines, causing severe leakage, which endangered his life. Yuqing saw through their scheming, had someone support him to sit and handle affairs, then summoned the malicious physician and killed him with a staff. After that, he went back to sleep and died before he could settle on the mat. He was buried on Hengshan. Even to this day, people fear him, and those who pass by his grave must lower themselves. Those suffering from fever would take soil from his grave and place it on their beds, and it would relieve their symptoms, demonstrating the high regard and fear in which he is held.[21p]
    "Records of Escaping Summer": Su Zizhan delighted in discussing immortals, and later came to admire Yao Danyuan, whom he thought to be a reincarnation of Li Taibai, dedicating dozens of poems to him. Yao was originally the son of the wealthy Wang family in the capital, but was disdained for his lack of virtue and was driven away by his father to serve a Daoist priest at the Jianlong Temple. He was naturally intelligent and read extensively from the Daoist canon, being able to recite it all. He also acquired many formulas and elixirs, largely speaking grandly and crafting poems filled with wild and unconventional phrases, enabling him to gain recognition. He floated through Huainan, often changing his name; at first, Zizhan was unable to distinguish him. Later, he reverted to his surname and named himself Wang Shi. During the Chongning period, when I was in the capital, he had already risen to the position of medical officer through his skills. He frequented the household of Duke Cai Lu, often engaging in strange medical practices. I still recall that he spoke with Duke Lu about Zizhan's matters, claiming that he could describe the palaces of the immortals on the sea, making them visible in the sky. Duke Cai also trusted him. When arranging matters in Chuzhou, Liang Shicheng sought Zizhan's letters and recommended his skills. At the end of the Xuān Hé period, he again became a Daoist priest, named Yuancheng. He vehemently attacked Lin Lingsu, who poisoned him, causing him to vomit blood and die.[22p]
    "Records of Dongpo": Pang Anchang was a physician, not focused on profit. When he obtained ancient books and paintings, he could not contain his joy. The Daoist from Jiujiang had mastered some of his techniques and used them in collaboration with me. Unable to repay him, I merely wrote a few pieces in running script. I also told him, "This is the story of Anchang; it must not be neglected." When Shen Liaozu fell ill, he sought the Daoist for treatment, but feeling he had no money and being poor at writing, he urgently asked me for help. I jokingly said, "You, with your bright literary talent, why don't you compose a couple of poems?" The two gentlemen, Pang and Hu, often visited me, and soon they would seek me out at the dry fish shop.

    "Records from the Mo Zhuang": Duan Chengwu was highly skilled in medicine, yet the nobility complained that he did not properly manage his selections for rewards. He resided in Yixing, and only those with power could compel his attention. Minister Zhai Gongxun, residing in Changshu, wished to see him, so he entrusted an invitation through the governor of Pingjiang, Liang Zhongmo. Finally, he came. One day, a wealthy man from Pingjiang fell ill and sought Duan's help. Duan said, "This illness can be cured with just a few doses of decoction, but it requires no less than five hundred or a thousand as compensation." The family initially agreed to half of that, but Duan brushed off his coat and left. Eventually, they complied, offering fifty strings as the fee for medicine. Duan then sought more, and the offer increased to a hundred strings before he was willing to dispense the medicine. As he had said, it worked, and he returned home with his earnings. On the way back, he had a dream of a figure in red garments who said, "The Supreme Deity has chosen you as a physician, yet you take bribes, showing no heart for helping others." He commanded that he be beaten on the back twenty times, and his attendants obeyed. Upon waking, he still felt pain in his back. People examined it and found bruises; shortly after returning home, he died.[23p]
    In "The Qingbo Magazine": Hui once heard his father's friend Xu Zhikang discuss the essence of pulse diagnosis, claiming that it could predict a person's fortune or misfortune. During the era of peace, the monk Zhi Yuan from the capital diagnosed the pulse of Wang Jinggong and said that he would surely have joy from a child passing the highest exams. At that time, Wang Yuyu was present and deeply disagreed. The following year, the prediction indeed came true as the child passed the exams. Because of this, Zhi Yuan boasted of his accuracy and approached Wang for a written commendation as a favor. Wang wrote: "Master Zhi Yuan remarkably diagnosed my father's pulse, knowing that his son would achieve fame; however, Wang Chengzhi doubted that such a thing was possible in ancient times. Zhi Yuan replied: 'In the past, the physician He of Qin diagnosed the pulse of the Duke of Jin and knew that his good minister would die. If the fate of a good minister could be discerned through the pulse of the Duke of Jin, then why would it be surprising to know about a son through the father?' The letter was roughly as such." Xu added: "This is not Wang Jinggong's writing; rather, it is merely his disciple exploiting the master's renown to boast and promote his own practice."[24p]
    In the "Cheng History": Yu Wen Zhonghui was at the central office when I happened to see him. We discussed the principles of the five elements and engaged in free conversation. There was a guest present who casually mentioned: "The tattoo doctor Wang Jing, after being whipped in the city yesterday, returned from afar. Now that he’s back, he has resumed his previous duties and briefly mentioned old matters. He claimed he had never been beaten, and when he bared his back to show someone, it was intact and blemish-free. I initially did not understand how this was possible, but later I saw another doctor who said that all the beatings left scars, and only when the skin was bitten at first and then treated with gold leaf would the scar dissolve immediately, suggesting a conflict between the natures of metal and wood." Zhonghui laughed and said: "In ancient times, there was someone who sold a medicine to cure callused feet in the market, and he would put up a sign at his door that read: 'For the Emperor's service.' Others laughed at the insignificance of this claim, and it reached the higher authorities who summoned and punished him, but later pardoned his foolishness. After he went out, he added four more words: 'Previously announced for a call.' Is this not in a similar vein? Who will you sell this to?" The guest also laughed but did not dare to respond.[25p]
    At that time, Zhonghui had not yet recognized Jing. Two years later, I was in the village and heard that Zhonghui had not risen, and I cried for his position. When the people from the capital arrived, it was said: Jing had indeed used purging poisons to treat his foot ailment, leading to great misfortune. The court learned of this and ordered the palace where Jing had returned to be searched, sparing him from punishment and exiling him to Yongxing.

    I then remembered when I was in the capital, that Fu, the author, and Yao, the official, both took Jing’s medicine and witnessed his demise. The author did not raise his hands or feet and still advanced with one cut of the guillotine, not escaping with his mouth before he passed away. One day, I was following the class of Jingling, and saw the official Yao at the gate of the hall, who said: "The phlegm and illness are newly healed." He had truly suffered greatly. He then said: "The royal physician actually cured me, the ailment is gone; several dou of phlegm were expelled. Now I just feel fatigued; otherwise, I am well."

    Hearing this, I was privately worried, thinking it might not be able to withstand it. Within a week, he indeed passed away. Alas! How harmful is medicine like this! Reflecting on past words, I weep for him and record the end, mourning for his mediocrity.[26p]
    In my youthful years, I entered Fujian and passed through Fuzhou, where I heard of a physician known as the Black Tiger King, who was wealthy and influential in the county. I inquired about him, and learned that he was a descendant of a family with a past reputation. His grandfather had lived in the capital and named himself after the Black Tiger Pill, thus the family was called the Black Tiger King. As for the present generation, they found favor with Emperor Gaozong, accumulating positions and remaining in service, well-regarded throughout the nation as a physician. Even when they faced demotion, their title still held allure.

    Initially, Qin Hui held power but had not yet made himself prominent. He significantly bribed those close to the Emperor to secure his favor, while this family maintained a genuine relationship with him. At the height of his power, their influence rivaled that of Qin Hui, and they openly displayed their might without concern for lower-ranking officials. Many generals shared familial ties with the emperor and, having the advantage in military matters, sought to connect with the King of Han, first expressing a desire to become his adopted son. Consequently, they led armies from Jinling, inspiring many to follow suit without raising suspicion.[27p]
    The cypress desired to elevate its kindred, not speaking of it itself, but frequently requesting to advance the faction of the former leader with the court. The former leader, taking advantage of the situation, also petitioned on the cypress's behalf. As the sons arrived at the extended hall, gold and purple filled the entrance, obscured by bribes and favors, while the market grew rich, oppressing the children of commoners to serve as concubines; the crimes were too numerous to record, yet under the protection of the city and society, none dared speak out for thirty years.

    In the sixth month of the Xin Si year of Shaoxing, the Sichuan man Du Xinlao was appointed to the Southern Platform, planning to strike but had not yet launched an attack. At the same time, border conflicts arose; the former leader first arranged treasures to flee to the south, causing unrest in the capital. The emperor heard of this and was displeased. Liu Wuzhong, the general of Jingkou, proposed a preemptive strike, advocating for military action. The emperor's thoughts remained hesitant and indecisive, while he himself wished to respond with force. The former leader, naturally timid, still hoped for the firm peace to secure his safety, and thus he subtly remarked to the emperor: the border regions had no real issues; it was merely new officials eager to display their military prowess for credit. If they were to execute one or two people, peace could be restored.[28p]
    The emperor did not respond, and Xu said to the attendant, "Does he want me to execute Liu Qi?" At that time, those who had been obstructed and unable to act were gradually influenced by it. Jin Liang demanded that our ministers send Xu Yi and Zhang Lun on an envoy mission. Liang used improper means and sent the advisor Han Rujia to stop them at Xuyi, and ordered them to send what was requested. When the memorial reached the emperor, he happened to be in the pavilion of Consort Liu, about to take a break from his meal. The consort was puzzled and inquired about it from the attendants, learning the reason. She proceeded to explain with broad examples, which somewhat corresponded with earlier discussions. The emperor was greatly shocked and asked, "How did you come to know this?" The consort could not conceal it and answered with all she had heard, which only intensified his anger. On Dingwei day, an edict was issued for the consort to return to her separate residence. Shen Lao subsequently submitted a memorial listing her ten offenses. Initially, when he read it, his face still showed displeasure. Shen Lao knocked on the platform and said, "As I enforce the law for Your Majesty, I cannot overlook even a physician; I would rather die than step back." He still did not grant permission.[29p]
    Due to secret discussions among outsiders regarding Ji Xian, claiming he had benefited from unorthodox methods, I feared that slander would arise, and I could not bear to listen. The Emperor began to change color and nodded in acknowledgment. He dismissed the court and ordered a proclamation stating: "I, with great benevolence, have granted you favor; thus, you have benefited. Such words as these will not allow you to see me again. Within three days, you shall have provisions for your own planning!" On the day of Xin Hai, he commanded Ji Xian to reside in Fuzhou, with his descendants' properties in the capital confiscated by the state; the strong servants and those sold off were allowed to be traded freely. After this order, there was great joy both inside and outside the court. Ji Xian had hidden many possessions and moved them far away, so even with confiscation, his wealth was not harmed. To this day, his former residence, adorned with magnificent beams and intricately carved eaves, is still called a grand estate. As his descendants spread apart, they could no longer sustain themselves. Some said his downfall came because he did not adhere to the proper way and thus was justly met with ruin. Zhao Xingzhi's "Records of the Restoration" meticulously details the beginnings and ends of Ji Xian, incorporating what he had heard about these events.

    In "Miscellaneous Records of Gui Xin": There were two physicians by the name Wang: Wang Jixian, the national physician during the reign of Emperor Gaozong, who later, for offering ineffective medicines at the De Shou Palace, was assigned to Fuzhou. Wang Jing was also of the same time as Ji Xian, serving in succession; later, while treating De Shou's illness, he prescribed cooling medicines but faced difficulties, ultimately returning home. The so-called imperial physician Wang Chengxuan refers to him.[30p]
    In the "History of Jin," the biography of Cheng Hui: Hui was appointed as a minister and was fond of eclectic knowledge, especially in discussing medicine. From Liu Shou-zhen of Hejian, he often used cool medicines. A prodigy who had added years to his life at a young age was summoned by Hui, who wrote the four characters "medicine is not trifling." The prodigy mistyped a character, changing it to "相" (xiāng). Hui felt quite ashamed, and people said he had hit upon his illness.

    In "Records from the Wild," it is said: Duke Wei of Xu suffered from severe boils and the emperor visited him several times. A large gathering of doctors treated him for a long time but his condition showed little improvement. The emperor suddenly granted him a meal, and the duke wept as he ate it, secretly instructing the physicians to flee. Not long after, he passed away, and the news was quickly reported to the emperor. The emperor, in disarray, mourned and rushed to the duke's residence, commanding the execution of the physicians. The duke’s wife wept greatly, and upon coming out to pay her respects to the emperor, he comforted her, saying: "Sister, do not worry for the future; I will take care of it." He then made arrangements for the aftermath and departed.

    In "Records of the Golden Terrace": Dai Yuanli of Jinhua was a renowned doctor at the beginning of the Jin dynasty. He was often summoned to Nanjing, where he visited a family of physicians. They welcomed him at their door and were quite busy responding to inquiries. Yuanli felt that their understanding must be profound, so he observed them closely. The prescriptions and medicines they prepared were all ordinary; he found this puzzling and visited them repeatedly. One day, someone sought medicine, and after leaving, Yuanli chased after him and advised: "When preparing, add a piece of tin." The man waved him off. Yuanli was greatly surprised. Reflecting that no one adds tin when preparing medicine, he asked and was told: "This is an ancient recipe." Yuanli found the source of this knowledge and it turned out to be just the character for syrup. He urgently corrected it. Alas! How can physicians, who cannot distinguish between syrup and tin, not be cautious in their practice?[31p]
    "Talks of Chang'an": It was customary in the Imperial Medical Academy that on the Dragon Boat Festival, officials would go to Nanhai to catch toads, prepare oil from them, and make purple gold ingots. One person made a grand affair of it, preparing drums, flutes, and banners, making a great din on the way. Someone mocked this in a poem saying: "Shaking off authority, I leave Fengcheng, surrounded by drums and flags, the vibrant banners shining. Entering the forest like a fierce tiger, I catch a toad and poke out its eyes."

    In the common folk, there were women skilled in medicine and pulse diagnosis, chosen from various departments to serve under the Imperial Medical Bureau. Those selected were honored with royal appointments, and many women took pride in this, calling them "medicinal mothers."

    "In the Dust": A famous doctor was about to enter Shu when he saw a man carrying firewood, sweating profusely while bathing in the river. The doctor said: "This man will surely die," but hurried to save him. The man went into an inn, took garlic, minced it finely, poured hot water over it, ate it, and sweated profusely like rain. The doctor remarked: "Even the poor know of medicine; what about the rich and noble?" Thus, he did not enter Shu.

    "In the Fei Xue Record": It is not advisable to consume wood frogs. A man from the Su family had two sons, both deeply loved, indulging them in their eating. They developed a chronic affliction. The father received a prescription that involved cooking wood frogs with pork. The younger son died that night, and the elder died the next day. The fool was careless, easily believing in false remedies, leading to the deaths of his two sons; how tragic! A friend, Ma Junwen, truly obtained a collection of medical prescriptions, which also included this prescription, thus noting this incident as a caution on the left. This is one aspect of benevolence.[32p]
    A boy had a sore on his head, and when someone applied medicine to it, the boy's head became unbearably itchy. He scratched until it bled and eventually struck his head against a post. By evening, he died. The medicine contained arsenic, which harms a person when it comes into contact with blood. I heard this from Wen Cheng.

    In the "Diary of Mountain Springs": There was a man named Li, living outside the gate of Yugan. He was skilled in commerce and daily sold spleen-nourishing pills in the market. He once hung a large sign saying, "If the cloves and sandalwood are not mixed, heaven will strike and the earth will be destroyed." He had two servant girls to assist him in preparing the medicine. One day, Li got drunk and drowned in the river. His family was unaware, but he did not return home for several days, prompting them to send trusted friends to search for him, but there was no trace. When the officials investigated, some reported his family, who hurriedly went to the site and found his body in a state of decay, only able to identify it with great difficulty. They wished to wash it but it was too late. Thus, they buried him in a place with piles of grass and erected a wooden sign at the grave that said: "The Grave of Doctor Li, the Merchant of Medicine." Someone later wrote on the back of the sign: "Li, the Merchant of Medicine, robust and discerning in trade. One day, heaven granted him a report, and he drowned in the Eastern Canal." Soon after, the family's finances deteriorated. His wife sent away the two servant girls, abandoned their home, and took her two children to serve others. Someone asked her, "Your husband was not negligent in his work; surely heaven should favor him. Why such harsh retribution?" On another day, her later husband got her drunk and questioned her. She replied, "The two servant girls I sent away were entrusted to the care of my late husband, one named Sandalwood and the other named Clove, but in truth, neither medicine was used. Thus, he received this punishment."[33p]
    Miscellaneous Records

    Ancient proverb: If one is sick and does not seek treatment, one often recurs to traditional Chinese medicine.

    From "Strategies of the Warring States": Zhang Chou said to Han Xiangguo: The reason why people appreciate Bian Que is that he treats swellings. If there were no swellings, no one would seek him out. Now, you, sir, are in favor of Pingyuan Jun, which is seen as adverse by Qin; yet it is your support for Pingyuan Jun that makes you adverse to Qin. I urge you to consider this carefully!

    From "Shizi": There was a physician named Qu, who was a good doctor in Qin. He treated King Xuan for carbuncles and cured King Hui of hemorrhoids, both successfully. When Zhang Zi's back was swollen, he instructed Qu to treat it, saying: "This back is not my own, so let it be managed as it is." After treatment, it was cured. Indeed, Qu was skilled in treating illnesses, and Zhang Zi entrusted him with the management. The body of the nation is also like this; one must be entrusted with authority before treatment can begin.[34p]
    "Han Zi": A good physician can suck out a person's wound, drawing human blood. This is not out of kinship in flesh and bone, but because of its beneficial nature.

    "Zhuangzi - Heaven and Earth": In the medicine of the Yu clan, there are ailments that are bald and afflicted. When ill, one seeks a doctor. A filial son administers medicine to care for his kind father, his complexion burning with color; the sage is ashamed of this.

    "Geng Sang Chu": Nan Rong Zhu said: When someone in the village is ill, the villagers ask about it. The sick can describe their ailments, yet even so, they may not truly be sick. If Zhu understands the great way, it's like drinking poison to treat an illness. Zhu wishes only to understand the classics of health. Laozi said: In the classics of health, can one embrace unity? Can one avoid loss? Can one know fortune and misfortune without divination? Can one be still? Can one cease? Can one set aside others and seek oneself? Can one be carefree? Can one be untroubled? Can one be like a child? A child howls all day without restraint; this is the utmost harmony: to hold on all day without twitching fingers, sharing its virtue; to gaze all day without squinting eyes, not looking outward. Acting without knowing where to go, residing without knowing what to do, flowing with things and merging with their rhythm—this is the classic of health. Nan Rong Zhu said: So this is the virtue of the ultimate person? It was replied: No. This is what is known as ice melting and thawing. The ultimate person engages harmoniously with the earth and communes with the heavens, not clashing over gain or loss, not finding strangeness in each other, not conspiring with one another, not engaging in matters with one another, coming and going carefreely—that is called the classic of health."[35p]
    In the "Essence of Mozi," Mozi said: "It is only that which can be practiced; it is like medicine. The root of a plant, the Son of Heaven consumes it to alleviate his illness; how can one say it is just the root of a grass and not consume it? Now, when a farmer presents his taxes to a great lord, the lord offers wine and grains as offerings to the deities and spirits; how can one say it is only something done by common folk and not partake? Therefore, even though they are of low status, in comparison to the farmer above, and the medicine below, can it be said they are not as good as the root of a grass? Moreover, has the sovereign also heard the tale of Tang? In ancient times, Tang was to visit Yi Yin and had the son of the Peng family serve as his charioteer. Halfway there, the son asked, 'Where is the lord going?' Tang said, 'I am going to see Yi Yin.' The son of the Peng family said, 'Yi Yin is among the lowly in the world. If you wish to see him, you should rather summon and question him, as he has received rewards.' Tang responded: 'This is not something you understand. Now, if there is a medicine here that enhances hearing and vision, I will surely advocate for it and force myself to consume it. As for Yi Yin in our state, he is like a good doctor or good medicine; if you do not wish for me to see Yi Yin, then you do not wish for my well-being.' Thus, Tang sent the son of the Peng family away and did not allow him to serve."[36p]
    In the "Zhou Mu Wang" chapter of "Liezi": The Qin people had a son named Shao, who was kind in his youth but became afflicted with a delusional disorder in his adulthood. He heard songs and thought they were wails; he looked at white and thought it was black; he savored fragrant food and thought it was rotten; he tasted sweetness and thought it was bitter; he mistook the wrong for the right. Wherever he directed his mind, the world was turned upside down—the four directions, water and fire, cold and heat—nothing was not distorted.

    Yang said to his father, "The gentlemen of Lu are skilled in various arts; will they be able to help him? Why don't you seek their counsel?" His father, traveling through Lu to Chen, encountered Laozi and mentioned his son's condition. Laozi said, "Do you not know the extent of your son's delusion? People today are all confused about right and wrong, muddled by profit and loss; many share the same affliction, yet none are aware of it. Moreover, one person's delusion is not enough to undermine a whole family; a family's delusion is not enough to turn an entire village; a village’s delusion is insufficient to topple a nation; a nation's delusion cannot bring down the world. If the whole world were to be as deluded as your son, you would be the one deluded instead.

    Who can correct the sounds of joy and sorrow, the colors and scents that define right and wrong? Additionally, my words may not necessarily be free from delusion; how can the gentlemen of Lu, who are themselves deluded, help to resolve the delusions of others? The grains of your honor are no better than a swift return home."[37p]
    "The Transmission of the Han Poems" states: In times of peace, there are no mute, lame, blind, hunchbacked, or deformed individuals; fathers do not weep for their sons, and brothers do not weep for their siblings. The way does not maintain the burdens of past care, yet each meets their end in sequence; this is the skill of a wise physician, hence harmony and stability. The method of dispelling ailments lies in no other but in employing the wise.

    The poem says: There are the blind, there are the blind, in the courtyard of Zhou. They are the remnants of Zhou's rule. The ruler's ailments number twelve; without a wise physician, no one can cure them. What are these twelve ailments? They are atrophy, stumbling, reversal, swelling, fullness, obstruction, blindness, agitation, breathlessness, paralysis, and turmoil. How does a wise physician treat them? By reducing burdens and lightening punishments, atrophy does not arise; by preventing the common people from suffering hunger and cold, stumbling does not occur; by preventing wealth from being hoarded, reversal does not happen; by ensuring granaries do not rot, swelling does not develop; by keeping treasury and storage full, fullness does not ensue; by not allowing ministers to indulge, obstruction does not manifest; by ensuring lower sentiments reach the upper echelons, blindness does not occur; by showing care for the lower classes, agitation does not transpire; by implementing laws diligently, breathlessness does not arise; by preventing resentment from below, turmoil does not manifest; by not allowing the wise to hide away, paralysis does not occur; and by preventing the people from singing and defaming, turmoil does not arise.

    The high officials and ministers are the core members of the ruler's body. If these core members are free from ailments, then the ruler is also free from ailments. Thus, without a wise physician, no one can heal. If everyone suffers from these twelve ailments and does not employ the wise, then the nation is no longer its own. The poem says: Many will be deeply entrenched and beyond remedy; ultimately, they will perish. Therefore, if the wise physician is employed, the common people will be free from ailments; how much more so the ruler![38p]
    In "The Chronicles of the Li Family," it is stated: In Lu there was a man named Gongsun Chao, who told people: "I can treat deficiencies. Nowadays our customs are deficient, and by using medicine, we can bring the dead back to life."[39p]
    In the text from "Kong Congzi": Zai Wo was sent to Qi and later reported to the Master, saying: "Liang Qiu had been bitten by a snake and took three weeks to recover. When in the presence of the ruler, the nobles and guests celebrated. The disciples and the gathered guests offered cures. One disciple remarked: 'The reason for presenting remedies is because of illness. Now that Liang Qiu has recovered, why do the scholars again offer remedies? Do they suspect that Liang Qiu will encounter another snake bite and therefore wish to apply them?' Everyone sat in silence with no reply. What do you think of this disciple's statement?" Confucius replied: "Your assertion is incorrect. A good doctor often experiences three failures before succeeding. Given that Liang Qiu encountered a snake bite and recovered, he must think of those who share his ailment and will certainly inquire about the remedies. For this reason, everyone is discussing different remedies, wanting to sell their knowledge to those in need. Those who mention their remedies claim they are good as a way to compare the quality of their cures."

    In the text from "Xin Yu": In the past, Bian Que lived in Song, but fell into disfavor with the ruler, and fled to Wei. In Wei, there was a person gravely ill nearing death. When Bian Que arrived at their home to treat him, the patient's father said: "My son is critically ill and will have a renowned doctor come to treat him; you cannot help him." He sent Bian Que away. Instead, he summoned a spirit medium to seek blessings and incantations, cursing Bian Que. The patient eventually died, and the spirit medium could not heal him. Bian Que was renowned as the best doctor in the world but could not compete with the spirit medium. Those who sought distant help overlooked what was near, hoarding vast resources while neglecting what was right in front of them; this is what it means![40p]
    The "Huainanzi" on the main principles states: Among all things in the world, nothing is more toxic than chicken poison. However, a good doctor can extract and preserve it, finding use for it.

    Miao Cheng states: Nothing is unused. The poison of heaven's might, black beak, and medicine; a good doctor uses them to save lives.

    A skilled craftsman gradually perfects their work; within the realm of tools, there is nothing that does not conform. A sage king governs the people, a master craftsman governs horses, and a doctor governs illnesses—all use the same materials but seek their respective purposes.

    When a patient is bedridden, a doctor uses needles and stones, while a shaman uses incantations and offerings; the goal of salvation remains the same.

    If a doctor cannot manage the illness, and the patient refuses medicine, that is folly.

    In the forest teachings: It is difficult for a good doctor to treat someone with the same ailment as a deceased person.

    A child worries for a father’s illness; the healer is a doctor.

    In human teachings: To worry only after trouble arises is like a sick person who, exhausted, seeks a skilled doctor. Even with the talent of Bian Que or Yu Fu, one still cannot be revived.

    In the teachings of the Tai clan: The reason Bian Que is esteemed is not merely for adjusting medicine according to the illness but for feeling the pulse and blood, to understand the origins of the sickness.[41p]
    In the "Records of the Grand Historian," the biography of the sun states: Jia Yi said, "I have heard that the ancient sages, who did not reside in the court, would surely be found among the physicians and diviners."

    In the "Explanation of Names," it elaborates on appearances: "Pulse diagnosis" is akin to skilled deception. When a physician feels another's pulse, they understand the nature of the illness and make a corresponding diagnosis.

    Explanation of diseases: "Rash" signifies diagnosis. If there is a knot in the energy, it can be diagnosed and discerned.

    In the "Book of Yue," it states: "If one dies without seeking a physician, or a nation falls without strategizing, they are merely inviting disaster upon themselves."

    In the "Discourses on Salt and Iron," it is said: "Bian Que cannot restore flesh and bones; neither can a minor official maintain a fallen state."

    Bian Que cannot treat diseases that reject acupuncture and medication; wise and virtuous individuals cannot correct a ruler who ignores admonitions and criticisms.

    What is valued in a good physician is their ability to discern information and expel evil energies, not merely their skill in inserting needles and piercing the skin.

    Officials are not considered good based on making many judgments; physicians are not deemed skilled based on making many incisions.

    Bian Que knows the origins of illnesses by feeling the pulse — when yang energy is strong, it depletes and harmonizes with yin; when cold energy is strong, it depletes and harmonizes with yang. Thus, the energies balance, and evil energies find no place to reside.

    However, an inept physician does not grasp the principles of pulse intricacies and the divisions of blood and energy, recklessly stabbing without benefit to the illness, causing only harm to the skin.

    Now, to reduce the excess and supplement the deficient, the rich become richer, and the poor become poorer. Strict laws and harsh punishments aim to curb violence and stop wickedness, yet wickedness persists. The implication is that it is not Bian Que's needles and stones that are in play; thus, the people fail to fulfill their roles.[42p]
    In the "Thoughts on the Worthy" from the "Essays of the Hidden Scholar": Those who share the same plight as the dead cannot thrive, and those who walk alongside a fallen nation cannot endure. Is this merely empty talk? How can we know a person is unwell? It is because they do not crave food. How can we know a nation is about to fall into chaos? It is because they do not value the worthy. Therefore, in the household of a sick person, it is not that there are no fine dishes, but the person is unable to eat them, which leads to death. In the government of a chaotic nation, there are not no virtuous people, but the ruler is unable to employ them, resulting in its downfall.

    To live on fine rice and drink sweet wine is to nourish life. Yet a sick person loathes these, thinking them inferior to beans, wheat, and drinking clear broth; this is an omen of impending death. To respect the worthy and employ the capable, to trust the loyal and accept advice, is the foundation of security. Yet a blind ruler despises this, believing it is inferior to flattering sycophants; this is a sign of imminent destruction. Laozi said: "Only the ailing are ill, thus they do not suffer." The Book of Changes states: "Their loss, their loss, is tied to the mulberry." Therefore, those who cultivate longevity begin with treating illness through medicine; those who nurture a realm begin by restoring order through employing the worthy, thus they can keep themselves secure and their nations thriving.

    The highest physician treats the nation; the next, the illness. Governing a nation is akin to caring for one’s own body; sickness pertains to the body, chaos pertains to the state. The body’s ailments await a physician for recovery; a nation’s chaos awaits the worthy for restoration.[43p]
    To govern the body, there is the method of the Yellow Emperor; to govern the world, there are the teachings of Confucius. However, when illnesses go untreated and chaos remains unaddressed, it is not due to errors in the acupuncture or medicinal practices, nor are the words of the Five Classics misleading; rather, it is because those who apply them are unworthy. If unworthy, then measures are not circular, standards are not square, ropes are not straight, and plumb lines are not level. Drilling will not produce fire, striking stone will not yield gold, a golden horse cannot be chased for speed, and an earthen boat cannot cross water. All these eight are aspects of Heaven's way, observable in form; if one is unworthy, still achieving nothing, how much less can one expect to wield the Way of governance and steer the hearts of the people, riding the six dragons? Governing the world without true worthies is akin to treating illness without good physicians. In treating illness, one must have ginseng, but instead receives pigweed; one must have ophiopogon, but instead steams common wheat. If one fails to recognize the genuine and mixes remedies, the illness exacerbates without realizing one has been deceived. Consequently, one mistakenly believes the prescriptions are insincere and that medicines are useless for the illness, thus discarding later remedies and daring not to ingest them, instead only seeking shamans—even if facing death, this is acceptable. Rulers should seek worthy individuals and respond with humility and truth, not to promote their own agenda, and should not draw true worthies into base positions. When the country is in turmoil, they do not realize they are deceived by the inferior. One thus mistakenly claims the classics are untrustworthy and that virtue is of no benefit for rescue, abandoning the true words and no longer seeking improvement, instead relying on common officials—even facing ruin, this is acceptable. Below the Three Dynasties, all used pigweed with steamed common wheat in their prescriptions, leading to increasing illness and eventual death.[44p]
    Treatise on Forgiveness: In treating illness, one must first know the strengths and weaknesses of the pulse and where the Qi is blocked; only then can a prescription be made, thus allowing for recovery and prolonged life. In governing a nation, one must first understand the sufferings of the people and the origins of calamities; only then can prohibitions be set, allowing for the resolution of deceit and the peace of the country.

    Record of Narrations: Buying medicine and obtaining a wild goose is a difficult task for a doctor.

    Words of the Law - Tribute to Li: Bian Que was from Lu, and many doctors were from Lu.[45p]
    In "Shuo Yuan": Three折肱 can make a good doctor.

    In "Shen Jian": Medicine heals; it is used to treat illness. If there is no illness, there is no need for medicine. Just as meat cannot endure excessive food, how can it endure medicine? Cold leads to heat, and excessive heat can cause stagnation of yin. The use of medicine must be appropriate, otherwise it becomes harmful. If the vital energy is balanced, then there will surely be harm. The same applies to acupuncture and fire. Therefore, those who nurture their nature should not take too much medicine; it is only important to manage it in moderation.

    In "Wai Shi": A scholar who does not assist others while seeking power is like a sick person who does not rely on medicine while seeking recovery.

    When Zhang Qiu conducted his duties, he inquired about the way to govern the people from a wise man. The wise man advised him, saying: "Why not heal?" Zhang Qiu asked: "What do you mean?" The wise man replied: "When the world lacks proper governance, the emperor suffers from a false sense of grandeur, feudal lords become negligent, the common people face hunger, high officials fall prey to greed, and those in power engage in corruption. Those dismissed from office court favor from influential powers with harmful agendas. A state is like a home; when there are dangers both above and below, turmoil and attacks will arise, and the nation will be in peril. If you wish to govern the people, healing is all that is needed." Zhang Qiu asked: "How do I heal?" The wise man replied: "You can use benevolence and righteousness as medicine, and governance and education as broth. First, heal the illnesses of the rulers, then address the ailments of the feudal lords and commoners. Would that not be virtuous?" Zhang Qiu responded: "This is the art of healing from the master; how can the disciple dare to do this?" The wise man concluded: "Fear not, for by healing the feudal lords, if they recover, the nation will not be in danger. If the feudal lords do not recover, it is not only a calamity for the borders but also a disaster for the country."[46p]
    From this, we can see that the relations of scholars with feudal lords are akin to the relationships of physicians with their patients; they cannot be separated. Yet the Master asks, is it not out of desire for the Jin people that I release myself? One might ask: How can one consider the scholar's interaction with the lords?

    In ancient times, there was a man from Zheng who suffered from a serious illness for three years without leaving his house. Someone advised him, saying: "Your illness is grave; should you not seek a good doctor to cure you?" He replied: "The market has no good doctors; what can I seek?" The advisor said: "There is a good doctor in Chu; if you offer him a hundred pieces of gold, he will certainly travel a thousand miles to help you." The man from Zheng indeed obtained the good doctor from Chu and was cured.

    Thus, the physician does not seek out illness to treat; rather, it is the ailing who seek out the physician. Therefore, for the scholar, it is likewise the feudal lords that seek him out; he does not seek the lords for his connections. My acceptance of the Jin king’s invitation is like the Chu physician receiving the gold from the man of Zheng. When the man of Zheng received the physician, he was cured; if the Jin king does not achieve a cure from me, am I not similar to that physician from Chu? It is both a matter of trust and distrust. Now, the party strife has again made things difficult at court, and the illness of the royal family seems more severe than that of Jin already![47p]
    The king of Shu was delighted to see the ambassador, but upon hearing of his illness, he suddenly became distressed and went to his door with a single cart.
