The ambassador knew the king had arrived and closed the curtains to rest, commanding Li Yuanhou to wait at the steps. The king, upon seeing Li Yuan, inquired, "Is the ambassador unable to come out?" Li Yuan replied, "He is unable." The king asked, "May I enter to see him?" Li Yuan answered, "He has already fallen asleep, wait until he wakes, then you may ask to enter." The king then asked, "Is it possible that the ambassador suffers from a pestilence?" Li Yuan replied, "Pestilence is not an illness." The king said, "Then what is it?" Li Yuan explained, "It is known that heaven considers a subtle deficiency as illness, and the earth regards collapse and overflow as illness. The Son of Heaven sees hidden severity as illness, while kings and nobles perceive arrogance and extravagance as illness. Scholars consider poverty and difficulty as illness, and high officials regard cruel flattery as illness, while common people see hunger and cold as illness. Thus, the ambassador's illness is difficulty. The state does not promote the worthy, leaving scholars in distress, confined on the roads, with many chariots and official robes accompanied by packs of wolves, and the rich surrounded by dogs and swine. From this perspective, how can a scholar be free from illness? Indeed, illnesses arise from subtle deficiencies and collapses, thus heaven and earth employ sages as healers; illnesses from hidden severity and arrogance prompt the Son of Heaven and nobles to utilize worthy individuals as healers; illnesses of poverty and difficulty lead scholars to seek guidance through dreams; illnesses of cruel flattery urge high officials to seek wise rulers; and illnesses of hunger and cold cause common people to rely on diligent officials as healers. Hence, the ambassador's illness is one that medicine and stones cannot remedy, and ghosts and deities cannot torment; neither cold nor heat can invade it. It shows in his countenance yet remains unseen in his decay, resonates in his voice yet is not visible in his sorrow. He sings with the drum and lute yet does not express his joy, sighs by the riverbank yet does not reveal his worries—this constitutes the ambassador's illness."[48p]
    "Central Argument": A person suffering from illness should not be made a doctor, and one who is impure should not be made to follow the rules, as they lack validity.

    "Thank You for the Medicine" by Liu Xiaochuo of the Liang Dynasty: A very small matter can cause a delay in growth. Renowned doctors take medicine from the city. Even though shamans observe symptoms, and famous doctors administer acupuncture, treasures such as pine nuts and jade broths—that is to say, one might be embarrassed by the multitude of precious offerings.

    "The Family Instructions of Yan": In the matter of medicine, achieving subtlety is exceedingly difficult. I do not encourage you all to set your lives on it. By understanding the properties of medicine, even a small blend can be used in emergencies at home; this is also a worthy accomplishment, as were Huangfu Mi and Yin Zhongkan.

    "Central Discussion": The ailment of the inner gate does not manifest as pain, itch, or distress of the body; feelings and wisdom may lead one to be unaware of the depth of the illness. However, when the period arrives, the vital energy is suddenly exhausted, thus illnesses of the inner gate can lead to sudden death, which is what Bian Que detested the most. This is because Lu Yi could not distinguish it, and those who encounter it cannot attack it.

    "Miscellaneous Records of the Court and Countryside": Each doctor claims that a tiger struck by a medicinal arrow will eat pure mud to detoxify; a wild boar struck will peck at the jiachi (a plant). If animals know how to administer detoxifying medicine, how much more so humans? Jiachi is the same as sand ginseng, as stated in the "Erya" which calls it zhìni.[49p]
    "Li Shi Kan Wu": A physician who can discern life and death from reading pulses, if not endowed by heaven, has achieved this through accumulated learning. Initially, there may be skill, but ultimately, effectiveness diminishes. Scholars argue that in the beginning, skill is linked to the ability to command life, while in the end, it is related to the limitations of numbers. I say: not so. In the beginning, when one often succeeds, they delight in accumulating wealth; their memory is sharp, and their diagnostic skills are keen. As time passes, however, the muscles tire, willpower wanes, and the effectiveness becomes rare. The distinction between beginning skill and lacking later becomes evident here. If it is merely about numbers and their ebb and flow, can we truly say it reveals understanding of principles?

    "Han Yu Zaoshuo": A good physician does not concern himself with whether a person is thin or fat; he only observes whether their pulse indicates illness. A wise strategist does not regard the stability or danger of the world but observes the principles of order and chaos. The world consists of people. Stability and danger equate to fatness or thinness. The principles of order are the pulse. If the pulse is healthy, being thin is not harmful. If the pulse is ill but one is fat, they are doomed. Those who understand this perspective comprehend what it means to govern the world. During the decline of the Xia, Yin, and Zhou dynasties, the princes rose and waged war incessantly. For decades, the world did not collapse due to the continuity of order. When the Qin unified the world and did not allow any power to the princes, they amassed troops and set fire to them. The world collapsed in just two generations due to the lack of orderly principles. Therefore, even if the extremities are free of illness, they cannot be relied upon; it is solely about the pulse. Even if the world is at peace, it cannot be a source of pride; it is only about the principles of order. Concern for what can be relied upon and fear of what can be boasted of — this is what the good physician and wise strategist call heavenly support. The "I Ching" states: "Look at the foot and examine the auspicious signs." A good physician and wise strategist will do this.[50p]
    "On the Waking Words of Pine and Willow": People consume the five flavors to nourish the five vital energies; when afflicted, one alters the flavor and the energy diminishes, thus remedies are employed to restore balance to the flavors. This is the way of humanity, where rulers and subjects, parents and children, nurture one another; mulberry and hemp, grains and millet support one another. When there are violent people born among them, infringing upon the weak and offending the noble, it is necessary to correct this through punishment. In severe cases where factions grow strong and rebel against authority, military action is required to subdue them, allowing for order among the people. If there is no affliction, then medicine should not be tried; if the violent cease, then punishment can be reduced and military force can be restrained. Indeed, sweet flavors and rich fats, when overconsumed, harm one’s vital energy; how much more so with medicine? And how much more with those who apply wrong methods?[51p]
    "What I Saw in the Eastern Valley": Nowadays, the cost of herbal medicine is several times more than before for the cheap and common varieties, and for the more precious and refined ones, it is also several times higher than before. Even when carrying gold through the market, one may search for it without success; who does not know how difficult it is to obtain genuine medicine? Anyone who sets up a shop to peddle fake medicine is foolish. And those who wish to cure their ailments by purchasing fake medicine are even more foolish. What then should we do? We should be more cautious about our food and clothing. In ancient times, people prioritized diet and healthcare: in spring they consumed more sour foods, in summer more bitter, in autumn more pungent, and in winter more salty, balancing with smooth and sweet flavors. They maintained moderation in their diet, walking thirty steps after meals, thereby avoiding the need for a pharmacy. Careful eating and dressing is essential. If someone were to suffer from a disease and insists on following the notion of not taking medicine from a doctor, the expensive herbal remedies would not harm them. Especially dutiful children and loving grandchildren who care for their parents at home—how could they bear to watch them suffer without providing help? If a family has resources, they can still find good remedies; but if one is poor, what can they do? The poor indeed find it hard to obtain quality remedies, and the rich, even if they find good ones, may not necessarily have good doctors. Reflecting on this, I look up to the heavens and wish: may all people in the world find peace and happiness![52p]
    "Record of Tranquility": To eliminate ailments, one must return to the root; when the foundation is solid, illness can be attacked, and medicine can be effective. To manage a family, one must correct oneself; when one’s self is upright, other families can be governed, and commands can be executed. Strengthening the root and uplifting oneself is not something that can be achieved overnight.

    "Miscellaneous Sayings of Aici": Aici served the King of Qi. One day, he appeared in court with a worried expression, and the king, puzzled, asked him about it. Aici replied: "I am unfortunate; my young child is ill, and I wish to report this, thinking that the king has no one to assist with these matters. Although I am in court, my heart is truly concerned." The king said: "Why not speak earlier? I have good medicine; if the child takes it, he will recover." He then instructed to provide it to Aici. Aici bowed in acceptance and returned, giving it to his son, who took it and died shortly after. On another day, Aici was deeply mournful, and the king asked the reason. He replied with sorrow: "Your servant has lost a child!" The king granted him gold to assist with the burial. Aici said: "A deceased child is not deserving of royal gifts, yet I have something to request." The king asked: "What do you seek?" Aici replied: "I only seek the method that was effective for my young child previously."

    "Expounding on Brocade and Dew": In medicine, there is a method of pressing and rubbing; the pressing involves using hands to knead the affected area, while rubbing involves rolling and massaging it. The character for 'press' should be derived from 'hand,' so its written form should represent pressing. In the "Jade Compendium," there is no character for 'press' under the hand section. In the "Broad Rhyme," the character for 'press' appears, but is derived from 'talent.' The character for ‘case’ is derived from 'wood,' noted as being related to 'table.'[53p]
    "The Deathbed Instructions of the Luan City": Wang Yanruo, an eye doctor, served under Zhang Wending. When Master Po presented him with a poem during a gathering at Wending's, he used metaphors as evidence, distinguishing himself with persuasive arguments and profound insights, leaving later scholars perplexed. Master Po was indeed keen on writing in this manner.

    "Notes from the Old Study Hut": Chen Ya wrote: Chen Ya has just passed the examinations this year, and the whole town is celebrating with Li Yatuo. Li is Ya's uncle and a doctor. In the North, those who forecast fortunes are called巡官 (Xunguan). Xunguan was a title for county officials during the Tang and Five Dynasties, possibly named because of their practice of traveling to sell their skills. However, people in the North refer to all doctors as Yatuo, without understanding the term.

    Shizang Yong, known for his medical expertise, once stated that contemporary people possess weak capabilities, thus relying on ancient prescriptions, many fail to cure diseases. It is not just humans; even medicinal stones and plants are weaker than ancient ones. Without doubling the dosage, effectiveness cannot be achieved, hence Shizang's preference for hot medicinals earned him slander. Other doctors spread rumors, claiming, "Shizang uses three buckets of fire from the eaves." Some feared him, while only Zhang revered his words. Whenever he saw friends storing medicinal elixirs, he would consume them without regard to quantity, sometimes without informing the host. The host would be alarmed and hastily warn against excessive intake, while Zhang laughed dismissively, yet it did not cause harm. This was due to a discrepancy in disposition, others could not replicate it. Ultimately, in the late winter, while lying on a stone, he ingested elixirs, and overwhelmed by the cold, succumbed to a hidden toxicity and died.[54p]
    "In the 'Bo Zhai Bian': A man from Shu, Shi Cang, made a name for himself in the capital with his medical skills. Another from Youyang, Chen Cheng, also became notable in medicine. However, Shi favored warming medicines while Chen preferred cooling ones. The good doctors of old would measure the strengths and weaknesses of a patient, observe the yin and yang of the illness, and then prescribe remedies, either to tonify or drain according to the condition. Yet these two hold their own biases, categorizing everything into hot and cold, and both gained renown in their time. Why? There is a saying: Shi Cang uses three dou of fire from the eaves, while Chen Cheng relies on a plate of ice in his chest. Those who take mineral medicines secretly use the power of the drugs to satisfy their desires, yet they often tend to avoid discussing it. When an illness suddenly arises, even if they wish to keep it hidden, they cannot. Wu Jingyuan from Wuxing took sulfur—few knew of it. Twenty years later, his son Tuo was made an official in Huating and died due to a back ailment, revealing the lingering toxicity that affected even his son. Should one not heed this?

    In the 'Jihu Lu': Xu Yinji, a physician, often said: The disease and the medicine must match; only one substance should be used to attack it for a quicker recovery. Nowadays, people are not good at feeling the pulse, but rather gauge illnesses with their emotions, often using excessive remedies hoping for efficacy. This is like a hunter who does not know rabbits, spreading nets across fields in the hope of catching one; the method becomes superficial. If one medicine coincidentally encounters another flavor that counteracts it, it cannot exert its full potency. This is a proof of difficulty in healing."[55p]
    From "Yunlu Manchao": In medical texts, the human pulse is described as having three parts: the cun, guan, and chi. The area beneath the high bone at the back of the palm is the cun, below this is the guan, and below that is the chi. From the high bone down to the point where the cun pulse can be felt, that is the cun; thus above the point at which the chi pulse can be felt up to the tip of the middle finger, isn’t that the chi? The ancients used the body as a measure, hence they expressed it in terms of the pulse. However, modern doctors merely bend the middle finger, using the ends of two lines as the cun, or they measure length with the hand on the side. Although they are not far apart, when asked how to name the pulse as cun or chi, they cannot answer.

    From "Bingta Wuyan": Yang Zhu had a friend, Ji Liang, who fell ill. His son consulted three doctors: the first was a doctor of the Jiao family, who said: "The illness arises after birth; we should attack its gradual development." Ji Liang remarked: "Many doctors say the same." The second was a doctor of the Yu family, who said: "The illness arises before birth; it is extremely difficult to eradicate." Ji Liang commented: "This is a good doctor." The third was a doctor of the Lu family, who said: "The illness originates from constitution before it takes form, uniting life and death as one." Ji Liang said: "This is a divine doctor." After their consultations, his ailment healed. Ji Liang summoned three doctors and his illness was cured. I spent three months with him, reaching an understanding of life and death, and my ailment healed naturally. If I were Ji Liang, I would still have external concerns.[56p]
    "Records of Fragmented Thoughts": Confucius was an expert in abscesses, and Zhao Qi was considered a physician of abscesses. According to "Sayings and Essays": Yong Sui is a person's name, which was misattributed to Zhao Qi.

    "View from the Cold Eaves": In the past, when Qin Huan died, his eldest son inherited his techniques, and his medical reputation rivaled that of Qin Huan. However, his younger brothers, unable to surpass him in skill, each sought to create new and extraordinary practices attributed to their father in hopes of outshining their brother. It was not that they did not love their brother; they believed that without distinguishing themselves from him, they could not be valued like their father. There was no resolution in the world for this. Later, the father from the eastern neighboring state found writings in Huan's pillow and presented them as evidence, and thus the eldest son's techniques began to spread throughout the world. The historian's son said: In any dispute, there must be evidence. If one does not have solid proof, can the case be heard? Therefore, in disputes about chickens, there are proofs of beans and grains; in disputes about cattle, there are proofs of release; in disputes about thieves, there are proofs of touching the bell. Otherwise, against the evidence of mouse teeth and sparrow beaks, who can win their argument? Is it merely to distinguish the medical techniques of Huan's three sons? Hence it is said: Simplicity does not heed. It is also said: Examine the truth of their offenses.[57p]
    "Notes on Comparisons": There are things that are effective for our use yet cannot be without drawbacks. A person with bad teeth takes苦参 (bittersweet herb) and their teeth recover, but their waist becomes heavy and cannot lift. Are there not similar examples in the world? The blue snake is poisonous, but its tail has the antidote; Angelica revives the blood, yet it also breaks the blood; Ephedra induces sweating, yet the root can stop sweating; sour jujube awakens from sleep, yet its kernels lead one to long for sleep; the mole cricket treats issues of urination before the waist but causes constipation afterwards; all is one thing, yet the applications differ. The same applies to medicine.[58p]
    Asafetida has an extremely foul smell, but when used in food, it can remove odors; the smell of rancid meat is strong, yet if placed among it, it can mask the foul odor, though the scent of raw meat is not usually appropriate. A gentleman who governs a country has the responsibility to promote what is beneficial and eliminate what is harmful; one must carefully consider the circumstances of人才 (talent) when employing or dismissing them, right?

    In the "Jianming Shu," it is said: In the world of medicine, when doctors see someone unable to eat or drink due to illness, they immediately say the spleen is not grinding. However, just note that waterfowl like ducks, geese, and chickens have no teeth in their mouths and cannot chew food; they need the spleen to grind it before they can digest. Thus, their spleens are all thick and hard. Humans, on the other hand, differ from other animals; they have teeth and can chew foods, so their spleens are soft and weak, primarily relying on the qi for transformation. When a patient has weak spleen and stomach qi, they cannot digest food, not because of lack of grinding. The "Jia Yu" states: for those that gnash and swallow, they have eight orifices and are born from eggs; for those that grind their teeth, they have nine orifices and are born from live birth. Eggs and live births are fundamentally different, and so are the spleens and stomachs. Yet, medical practitioners do not comprehend this principle and speak generally, despite many through history, none have gained insight into this matter.

    In the "Yu Xiao Ling Yin," it is recounted: In the past, Duke Wen's two servants entered into the vital organs, and Bian Que identified it; Empress Cui of the Qin entered the spiritual realm, and Xu Zhi Cang recognized it. This was not due to an examination of their ailments, but rather a diagnosis of their hearts.

    In "San Yu Zhuibi": It is said that old doctors are valued more than young diviners, as the former are esteemed for their experience while the latter are prized for their youth. Old doctors all understand this, yet when asked about the meaning, young diviners cannot explain it. According to Wang Yanfu's "Chen Shi," age brings experience, while youth brings decisive action. Thus, we know that this saying has existed for a long time.[59p]
    "Chang'an Guest Talk": In the Taiyuan Medical Office, there is a bronze figure, hollow and filled with water, with all its key points open. It has an ancient, verdant color, shimmering and dazzling, said to have emerged from the tide of the sea.

    "Records of the Unusual": When a disease has reached a critical point, bitter medicine is ineffective, hence the use of acupuncture and moxibustion. Needles are effective for promoting circulation and alleviating stagnation, while moxibustion is for enhancing the flow of qi and blood. The ancients referred to it as the fire of longevity, also called the three contemplations of fire.

    "Snow Wave Novels": It is said that in a certain area, there was a person with a painful foot ulcer, unbearable in its agony, who told his family: "Carve a hole for me in the wall." Once the hole was made, he extended his foot through it, reaching into the neighbor's house by a foot or so. The family asked: "What does this mean?" He replied: "Let it be so, I will endure the pain in the neighbor's house; it has nothing to do with me." There was also a physician skilled in surgery. A soldier was shot by an arrow, the shaft penetrating deeply into the membrane; he brought it to the physician, who took the scissors from Bingzhou and knelt to request treatment. The soldier said: "The arrowhead is embedded in the membrane and must be treated urgently." The physician replied: "This is an internal matter; I did not expect to be held responsible." Alas! Though the foot goes into the neighbor's house, it is still my foot; though the arrow is within the membrane, it is still the physician's matter. Yet one desires to partition a foot through a wall and separate disciplines between membranes; how then can pain cease? How can accountability be evaded?[60p]
    Once there was a physician who claimed to be able to treat hunchbacks, saying: "Like a bow, like a shrimp, like a curved ring, extend my treatment, and one can be cured in the morning and stand straighter by evening." A man believed him and sought treatment for his hunchback. The physician took two boards, placing one on the ground and having the hunchback lie on it, then placed the other board on top and pressed down. The hunchback straightened, but also died. The man's son wanted to report this to the authorities, but the physician said: "I aim to straighten hunchbacks; I don’t concern myself with whether people live or die." Alas! In the world today, those in power only care about taxes and resources, indifferent to the lives of the common people. How is this different from that physician? However, if not for the presence of a wise ruler who exercises restraint and implements benevolent decrees, could one expect the officials to be any better than a hunchback's physician?

    In the "Qiu Xun" chapter: When treating an illness with medicine, once the patient is well, they should stop taking medicine. If they continue, it will only accumulate and cause illness again.

    In the "Qiu Zhi" compilation: Nothing is more significant in governance than the military and punishment, and nothing is more important for the people's livelihood than medicine. Thus, the Zhou officials had detailed provisions for inquiries, pardons, and forgiveness, avoiding arbitrary punishment; Tan Shi discussed military preparedness, ensuring no defeat in battle; oversight of successes and failures, eliminating the need for ineffective physicians. Now, in the Supreme Court's criminal department, with individual perspectives, those supervising military operations lack strategic foresight, and the people's health programs are neglected. How can one expect to promote governance that truly cares for the people?[61p]
    Outer Compendium

    "Private Thoughts on Herbalism": During the time of Shennong, common people sought medicinal beasts. When someone fell ill, they would caress the beast and convey words to it. The words were as transmitted by the common people, and their meaning was unknown. Once the words were spoken, the beast would immediately act like it was in the wild, picking a herb with its mouth, crushing it for juice, and taking it to recover. Later, the Yellow Emperor commanded Feng Hou to record which herbs arose for which diseases; after a long time, the formulas were fully verified. There is an ancient saying that the Yellow Emperor once tried all the herbs, which is not true. Therefore, Yu Qing said: the Yellow Emperor learned medicine from the beasts.

    "The Questions of Tang" in Liezi: Duke Hu of Lu and Qi Ying of Zhao both fell ill and sought treatment from Bian Que. After Bian Que treated them, they both recovered. He said to Duke Hu and Qi Ying: "Your previous ailments were caused by external disturbances affecting your internal organs, due to the effects of herbs and stones. Now there is a coexisting ailment that ages with the rites; what would happen if we attacked it together?" The two replied: "We wish to first hear your findings." Bian Que told Duke Hu: "Your will is strong, but your vitality is weak, which makes you full of plans but lacking in decisive actions; Qi Ying has a weak will but strong vitality, leading him to think little yet be harmed by fixation. If we exchanged your hearts, then both would attain balance in virtue." Bian Que then made the two drink poisoned wine, which caused them to be in a stupor for three days. He opened their chests and examined their hearts, switched them, and administered divine medicine. Once they awoke as before, the two departed for home. Duke Hu returned to Qi Ying's home and found his wife and children, who did not recognize him; Qi Ying likewise returned to Duke Hu's home where his wife and children also did not recognize him. The two households then had a dispute, seeking judgment from Bian Que. Bian Que discerned the cause of their confusion, and the dispute was resolved.[62p]
    In "Records of Qi and Xie": Xu Qiufu from Qiantang was skilled in treating illnesses and lived east of the Hu Gou Bridge. One night, he heard pained moans from the air. Qiufu got up and went to the source of the sounds, asking, "Are you a ghost? Why do you suffer so? Are you hungry and cold and in need of food and clothing? Do you need treatment for your illness?" The ghost responded, "I am from Dongyang, my surname is Si and my name is Sengping. I used to be a fun-loving official but died from severe腰 pain. Though I am a ghost in Hubei now, my suffering is as great as when I was alive. I came to inform you because you are a good doctor." Qiufu said, "But since you are formless, how can I treat you?" The ghost replied, "Just bind some grass to form a human figure, apply acupuncture to my腰 at two points, and then cast it into the flowing water." Qiufu made a grass figure, applied needles at the two points on the腰, offered a thin sacrifice, and sent it into the river. When night fell, he dreamed of the ghost, who said, "I have been relieved. I also accept the food offered; thank you for your kindness." Qiufu then became an official during the Song Yuanjia period.[63p]
    In the "Record of the Times" (朝野佥载): During the Jiushi years, a man named Yang Yuanliang from Xiangchuan, over twenty years old, observed at Wenshan in Qianzhu and dreamt one day of the Celestial Lord who said: "My hall is damaged; you will rebuild it for me and you are capable of healing all diseases." Upon awakening and understanding this, he tried healing, and there was no case he could not cure. In Ganyuan County, a local official had a swelling as large as a fist on his back; Yang used a knife to cut it, and after several days, it healed. Daily, he earned a thousand in money from healing. He completed the construction of the Celestial Lord’s hall, but gradually, the effectiveness dwindled.

    In the year of Ruyi, Zhao Yuanjing from Luozhou fell ill and, after five days, recovered, saying he saw a monk with a wood over a foot long, who taught him: “For those with illness, if you support yourself with this wood, you will be healed.” Yuanjing managed to find a similar length of wood and tried healing with it, which worked remarkably well. His door was crowded with hundreds of people daily. Inspector Ma Zhiji, noticing the gathering crowd, pursued a ban against this practice at the left platform, as patients filled the platform doors. Upon hearing this, Empress Wu summoned him into the inner court; there, government officials with ailments were healed by leaning on it. He was allowed to treat the people, and after several months, he earned over seven hundred strings of cash. Later, as efficacy declined, the practice eventually ceased.[64p]
    "Records of the Divine Inquiry": Chen Zhai, a shaman from Jinjiang, Quanzhou, excels in the techniques of exorcism and has successfully treated many illnesses. There was a traveler from Zhangzhou named Su Meng, whose son was afflicted with madness that no one could heal. So, he went to seek Chen's help. When Chen arrived, Su's son saw him and shouted curses while pointing at him. Zhai observed, "This sickness has entered his heart." He set up an altar in the hall, warning everyone not to peek. At night, he took Su's son and split him in half, hanging the body on the east wall of the hall, with the heart suspended under the northern eaves. While Zhai performed his rituals in the hall, the heart was eaten by dogs. Zhai, unable to find it, became alarmed and, wielding a knife, turned around and left. The hosts were unaware and thought he was merely performing a ritual. After some time, he returned, holding the heart and entered the sick man's belly, his hair disheveled and shouting, causing the abdomen to come together. Su's son regained consciousness, repeatedly calling out "Pú pú." The family could not understand. A few days later, while traveling several miles from home, a postal official holding government documents died by the roadside. Originally, there were relay stations along the southern postal route every twenty miles, where postal officials passed on messages with talismans, and they would call out warnings to approach the next station. Zhai took the postal official's heart and revived Su's son, who fully recovered.[65p]
    In the "Records of Dongpo," Wang Fangyuanlong said: Qian Zifei had an effective remedy for severe wind disturbances and often administered it to others. One day, he dreamt that someone told him: "Heaven has decreed this illness for a man, and if you go against Heaven by continuing to treat, you shall suffer this illness yourself, and no medicine will be able to save you." Zifei, frightened, decided not to administer the remedy. I believe that the ailments decreed by Heaven cannot be treated, and therefore the medicine should no longer be effective. If the medicine is effective, it suggests that Heaven cannot inflict illness. This is an example of the affliction fearing the medicine, using Heaven as a means to prohibit human action. The sickness of Duke Jin was caused by two young boys, and Li Ziyu’s red pills were also revealed in dreams, indicating that there was some external influence. Zifei, not recognizing this, was coerced by spirits. However, if it were me, I would think differently; if a patient could recover, I would willingly bear their suffering. My family has a method that can be applied to the skin, which can cleanse the foulness from within the abdomen. It was tried in Huangzhou, and the illness healed well; now I intend to regularly administer it to others.[66p]
    In the "Recent Records of What Is Heard and Seen": Zhang Wendin was stationed in Shu. On the Double Ninth Festival, while attending the medicine market, he took shelter from a sudden morning rain beside Zhang Ziyang, an itinerant physician, at the Yujuguan. Soon, the skies cleared, and a white-clad old man stood among the trees. He turned to Ziyang and said, "I have something for you to communicate to your master." Ziyang nodded in agreement. The old man then handed out two medicinal pills, as small as millet, for Ziyang to deliver to Wendin. Ziyang asked, "Do you know Yin? What is your surname?" The old man replied, "My surname is Ge. I have served as a vice minister and have been stationed in Shu for two terms. I have seen him before." Ziyang noted, "This is your only visit, then." The old man said, "Tell your master that I will come again another day." Ziyang took the medicine and reported everything to Wendin. He took one grain of the mercury, combined it with one tael of the medicine, and soon it made a sound, like a distant bell, clear and extraordinary. Listening closely, a round light suddenly emerged from within, illuminating the entire room. Startled, he took it, and the mercury transformed into gold. Wendin then ingested the remaining pill, sending someone to inquire again, but he could not be found.[67p]
    "Longchuan's Reminder": Zhang Andao, knowing about Chengdu, followed as a medical officer. On the Double Ninth Festival, he was invited to watch the medicinal market. At dawn, it began to rain as the market closed, so he entered the Jade Pavilion to take shelter. Upon reaching the palace, he saw a Daoist sitting at the steps. He approached and inquired about his well-being. The Daoist said: "Zhang Duanming has already been in Shu for the second time." The physician replied: "He has only just come to Shu." The Daoist responded: "You do not know. The average person's vital energy is sixteen taels; as one ages, it diminishes, and Zhang Gong has lost over half." I have known him well for a long time, and now to see you is not coincidental. He took off his clothes, presenting two circles of the medicine, saying: "One circle can replenish one tael of energy." The physician said: "Although Zhang Gong is fond of the Dao, he is inherently cautious and may not believe it." The Daoist replied: "The reason for the two circles is precisely for you." He took one circle and one tael of mercury, put them into a furnace, covered it with a cup, and burned it for a long time, with a sound like cracking wood. After lifting the cover and tossing in some resin, he felt something unusual; after three tosses, the medicine was complete. One should know this is not an ordinary medicine. The physician returned directly, reported to Gong, and tested it as advised: every time he tossed in the resin, flames leaped up, illuminating the small pavilion where they sat. By the third toss, the flames were golden, and when poured out, they appeared purple-gold. He then consumed one circle and sent the physician to explore Chengdu, hoping to encounter it again. Later, he saw it again in front of the Kongming Temple and obtained another circle of medicine, but consuming it brought no other change.[68p]
    "In the 'Records of Divine Encounters': In Jiangnan, there was Zhang Yaoqing, the governor of Jizhou, who had a strong attendant named Tao Jun. Jun was naturally cautious and straightforward. He had once served in the military and suffered an injury from a falling stone, which left him with ailments in his waist and legs, so he always relied on a cane to walk. Zhang ordered him to guard a boat at the mouth of the Guangling River. One day, while taking shelter from the rain in a tavern at Baisha Market, many people gathered there. Two students approached and spoke to Jun, saying, 'This man has a good heart; he should seek treatment for his ailments.' They then called Jun over, gave him two pills, and said, 'Take these, and you will recover.' After they left, Jun took the pills back to the boat. After a while, he felt intense pain in his abdomen, but soon the pain stopped, and his ailments greatly improved. He managed the oars and ropes with newfound ease and strength. Baisha is eighty miles from the city, yet he made the round trip in a single day without feeling exhausted. Later, he inquired about the two students but could not find them again, leading people to suspect they were divine beings."[69p]
    In Jiangnan, a government official named Shen Zhang Yi was suffering from a fever and was in distress, feeling extremely bewildered. He saw a divine figure, about several inches tall, standing before his pillow, holding three pills and saying, “Swallow these, and you will recover.” Yi took the pills and quickly swallowed two of them, while the third one fell to the ground with a sound. He then got up to look for it, but couldn’t find it. His family, startled, asked what had happened. He recounted his vision, and his illness was immediately cured. That same day, he went out into the streets with no suffering at all.

    The "Records of Cao Prefecture" state: In the early years of the Jiajing era, a local person named Wu Shiyu Kai, who was only a few years old, was afflicted with a severe illness that caused him great torment, and he felt there was no hope for salvation. One night, he suddenly had a dream where a man in a yellow cap came to him, urging him to get up. The man then used a knife to make an incision in his side, removing his intestines and stomach, cleaning and washing them, and then putting them back, applying an ointment on the outside. When Kai woke up, he immediately felt an extraordinary coolness and relief in his chest and was so amazed that he told his father about the dream. His father, touching his abdomen, found that most of the mass had already subsided. Elated and pondering, he said, “Surely, a divine healer has shown you mercy.” The next morning, he took Kai to the shrine of the Three Sovereigns, honoring various famous physicians, and, upon seeing the statue of the Real Man Sun, he pointed and said, “This is the one who treated my illness in the dream.” His father then offered sacrifices and renewed the shrine, and Kai was free from his ailment. After passing the imperial examinations, whenever he went on official tours, he would always visit the shrine to pay his respects, looking after its beams and pillars, maintaining and adorning it for the rest of his life.[70p]
    In the "Chronicles of Pingyang Prefecture": Yang Dong, courtesy name Shizi, was a man from Puzhou. He was intelligent and well-read, excelling particularly in medicine. The magistrate He Lingbao once summoned him to the bedchamber of a young woman whom he suspected was unwell, instructing him to examine her. After checking her pulse, Yang declared that she was a healthy young man without any ailments. This caused great surprise. After his death, he practiced medicine at the transport office in Hedong, and those who recognized his face would exclaim, "Isn't this Yang Shizi from Puzhou?" He then stopped being seen. One day, Zhai Bing from Shuiwen Village in Ruicheng visited Puzhou and went to the pharmacy to inquire about someone’s name. His son replied, "That is my deceased father. Why do you ask?" Bing said, "I had a previous agreement to visit your father on the 9th of this month." At that moment, Guo Weiliang and others questioned him about it, and Bing replied, "I am an old physician who treated patients in my village." The crowd was astonished, suspecting that he was a spirit.[71p]
    "Liu Yunshan was a doctor during the Wanli period. At that time, his skills were not well-known, and he died thirty-seven years before his name began to be recognized. Chen Ziwun heard this and said: 'How strange! Can this be true?' A guest replied: 'In Hangzhou, there was a wealthy family whose member was gravely ill and on the verge of death. His family had already gathered to mourn him when a doctor suddenly appeared, saying, 'I am Liu Yunshan.' After examining him, the patient recovered. The doctor refused the gold offered in gratitude and said, 'We shall meet again in Piling City, in the alley of the Temple of the Minister.' A month later, the wealthy family’s son sought out Liu Yunshan, but an elderly man in the alley said: 'You are mistaken! Liu Yunshan has been dead for thirty-seven years.' However, during his lifetime, Yunshan believed in the gods and once dreamt that he was instructed by the god of this temple to collect money to expand the shrine, and he even had an image of himself created beside the deity, which still resembled him. The son of the wealthy family leapt forward, looked at the statue in shock, and left, weeping. From then on, people from our county would come to observe, worship, and pray, with daily crowds and apparent miracles occurring. Chen Ziwun heard this and said: 'How strange! Can this be true?' Nevertheless, I believe that had Yunshan's skills been known during his lifetime, he would have fully realized his ambitions. Being burdened with his skills but not having the chance to showcase them, this is likely why Yunshan refused to be forgotten even in death. Though the story might seem bizarre or incredible, a gentleman should also empathize with his aspirations and give it some credence."[72p]
    In Shu, Liu Wenji recounted to me that in the past there was a so-called "Old Immortal" among the bandits, a remarkable figure who could bring dead people back to life and reattach severed limbs and bones. The bandits revered him as if he were a deity. Initially, when he was captured, they intended to kill him. However, they did not execute him immediately but instead spared anyone who had any skill or craft. The immortal was called "the sculptor" because he managed to be exempted thanks to his ability to sculpt figures out of clay.

    One day, the sculptor was cleaning a large cauldron, pouring water and dismantling a house for kindling. The water boiled, bubbling over several times, and with a stick, he stirred it into a paste. The bandits were astonished and spread the word. When the leader of the bandits heard about it, he thought he was a sorcerer and decided to kill him. The sculptor said, “Allow me one word before death. Does the king not wish to accomplish great things? Why then kill an unusual man?” The bandit leader, intrigued, asked him to explain. The sculptor replied, “I possess a strange art: I can bring people back to life. This paste is granted by the immortals; whether by knife or axe, or even a severe injury, I can restore the severely wounded to full health in an instant.” The bandit leader then struck a person to test it, and it was immediately verified. The bandit leader was cruel and regularly killed people, inflicting various tortures without count.[73p]
    Hundreds were flogged, their flesh and blood torn apart, barely alive. Those who were still breathing were handed over to the sculptor, who applied a white paste to them. All of them revived and were able to walk immediately afterward. In the army, many rushed to seek them, and every day food and drink were generously offered, thus gradually filling their clothes and bags.

    One of the captured bandits had a favored general, who was struck by a flying stone during an attack and had his chin severed, barely breathing. The sculptor said, "It's easy." He then cut off a person's chin and pressed it against the general's, applying paste; within a day, he recovered and could eat and drink as if he had never been injured.

    At that time, Sun Kewang was overseeing the bandits as military supervisor. One night, inebriated, he killed a favored concubine, and by morning marched twenty miles, waking up filled with regret. On the way, he encountered the sculptor and jokingly asked, "Was the military supervisor drunk last night? Why does he not look well?" Kewang explained the situation, and the sculptor replied, "Does the supervisor still think of that person? I will turn back and search for her." Kewang said, "Alas! When the camp broke, the body was gone; I imagine it has been devoured by dogs or pigs. Where could we look?" The sculptor said, "If the supervisor orders me to search, what would dogs or pigs dare to eat a noble person's body?" Kewang responded, "Find me a rat! Are you trying to escape? I will send an officer to escort you to search." The sculptor laughed and said, "What place is there to search? What could we possibly find?" Kewang grew angry and exclaimed, "Are you mocking me?" The sculptor pointed to a nearby bundle and said, "No need to search; right here it is."[74p]
    Ke Wang said: "What use are rotting bones? Why carry them?" The sculptor laughed and said: "What does the supervising officer say?" Ke Wang dismounted and unfurled his mat, his starry eyes swirling, looking as melancholy as rain-laden pear blossoms, for the soul within the tent had already returned. Ke Wang rejoiced and cried out, startling the entire army, which was heard by the bandits. The bandit leader said: "This is a divine being! We shall confer an honor upon him." Fearing that the masses would not understand, he ordered in the large camp by the Great Marsh that each soldier prepare a table for gathering at the vast plain the next day. At that time, the bandits numbered in the hundreds of thousands, and they prepared hundreds of thousands of tables, the highest one designated for worship of the divine being.

    Then the sculptor was dressed in a deep robe, with a silk scarf on his head, and shoes with silk laces. The sculptor was six feet tall, broad forehead, wide face, with a beard, resembling the gods depicted in society. However, when commanded to ascend the platform, which was high and precarious, the sculptor hesitated and did not wish to climb. The bandit leader ordered the soldiers to each draw their bows with arrows aimed at him, saying: "If you do not climb, we will shoot!" The sculptor had no choice; when halfway up, trembling in fear, he felt like he was a target for a myriad of arrows, yet dared not stop, and forced himself to reach the top.

    The bandit leader commanded the three armies to lower their bows and arrows, lining up to bow below, calling him "Old Immortal" three times. At that moment, the sound echoed through heaven and earth, and from then on, they no longer called him the sculptor, but all referred to him as "Old Immortal." From this point, the Old Immortal also no longer lightly tried his craft.[75p]
    There was a certain bandit who, after a battle, was injured and had a broken leg. The only thing that remained intact was a small piece of skin. He sought treatment from an old immortal, who refused, saying it was not easy. The bandit cried out in sorrow, presenting gold and silk as offerings, but the old immortal waved them aside, saying: "These are external possessions; I have no need for them. However, I cannot bear to see your injury. I have no son; can you take care of me?" The bandit swore by heaven that he would serve him as a child for life. The old immortal calmly opened his pouch, took out a small saw, and sawing off about an inch from both ends of the injured leg, took a living person's leg bone, measured it, and connected the two, applying medicine. In just a few days, he was healed. From that point on, anyone in the bandit group who sought his medicine dared not offer extravagant gifts, and all vied to become his adopted son.

    Among the bandits, there was a favored servant girl known as "Old Foot," beautiful and intelligent, skilled in calligraphy and painting, though her feet were not very slender, so she was named. She was in charge of all communications and written orders among the bandits and was favored by the bandit leader. One day, the bandit leader saw her sitting alone, and "Old Foot," not wanting him to think she was with someone else, approached to serve him. The bandit leader, unaware it was "Old Foot," suspected others were watching and struck her with his dagger, hitting her at the waist, breaking her bones and cutting open her abdomen, killing her. When the bandit leader realized, he was filled with regret and sorrow and urgently summoned the old immortal. The old immortal said: "She is already dead; there is nothing to be done." The bandit leader scolded: "Old cunning one, wasn't she one of the guards? You too, shall die for not being able to save her!" The old immortal hesitated and said: "It will take time to solve this."[76p]
    The traitor Xiang desperately wanted to preserve his life and set a deadline of three days. The old immortal requested a period of thirty-seven days. They combined wine with medicine and poured it into him. A sound like a clattering came from his throat when a dagger was used. The old immortal congratulated him, saying he could be saved and would recover in seven days. They then took water to moisten his intestines, filled his belly, stitched him up with a needle, applied medicine, reinforced him with wooden boards, and bound him with ropes. Indeed, after seven days, the old man walked as he normally would. When the traitor died, his followers scattered, fleeing from Shu to Dian. Those who had always respected the old immortal protected him and accompanied him to Dian.

    When the Yongli reign began, many of the followers posed as false nobility. The old immortal boasted among the nobles, accumulating wealth, and set up a residence in the eastern corner of the city. He piled stones to form a mountain, dug a well to create a pond, planted flowers and trees around, and raised hundreds of red fish. When guests came, he would float white fish out of the water for amusement and, when drunk, would sing loudly and lie down, completely unconcerned. Until Yongli fled to Myanmar, the old immortal followed him. When they reached Tengyue, he often faced the sky, as if he had something to express. One day, he said to Wenji: "I am old! What should I do?" Wenji replied: "Just wait for death, what do you care? But your unique skills, which you have never shared with others, will be lost. That is a pity." The old immortal said: "I do not refuse. My master gave me warnings when he taught me."[77p]
    Upon inquiring about the reason for his situation, he replied: I am a man of the Chen surname, from Dengzhou in Henan, born to a scholarly family. In my youth, I attended the local academy, but I was not fond of the classic texts. There was a statue of gods and Buddhas beside the school, which I often played with. The schoolmaster would occasionally reprimand me, and when I returned home, my parents would scold me for not studying, which I could not endure, so I decided to run away, feeling lost and with nowhere to go. I prayed to Guandi and received an oracle that said: "In the future, you shall stand shoulder to shoulder with kings and lords." Looking at myself, a lost child of a fallen family, how could I stand shoulder to shoulder with kings and lords? Yet, the divinity did not deceive me; the only ones who stand shoulder to shoulder with kings and lords are the immortals. I had long heard that there are many hidden immortals in Zhongnan Mountain, so I wished to follow them. Enduring hunger and cold, I searched everywhere but could not find anyone to learn from. One day, I reached the back of the mountain and, from a distance, saw a cave in the sheer cliffs, with people entering and exiting. I pushed through the thorns, climbed the craggy rocks, and reached the cave, where I saw a Daoist sitting on a stone, serene and unlike ordinary people. I was fortunate to think: This must be my master! I thus knelt and begged for instruction. The Daoist did not look at me and waved his sleeve, returning to the cave. I did not dare to enter, so I bowed at the cave entrance instead. This happened for three days, when suddenly a young boy appeared, holding something and showing it to me, saying: ‘The master has sent you this to eat.’ It resembled a cake, was white, about two inches square, and tasted sweet like honey. After eating it, I was no longer hungry. I secretly rejoiced and became more convinced, continuing to worship and request guidance.[78p]
    On the seventh day, the Daoist suddenly came out and asked me, "Fool! What do you wish to do?" I replied that I sought to become an immortal. The Daoist laughed and said, "Go! You are not one of us, why do you torment yourself?" I thought to myself that I had no place to return to, and that I might as well die by throwing myself off a cliff, so I wept and pleaded. After a while, the Daoist said, "I see you are sincere. Here is a book; I will give it to you along with a lifetime's worth of provisions. Do not take it lightly, for if you reveal it, you will be struck by lightning. Leave quickly, and do not linger, for you will only be prey to tigers and wolves." I received the book with surprise and joy, hurriedly descending the mountain. Upon examining it, I found it was a collection of forbidden methods, about thirty pages long.

    In Yan'an, news spread that a certain governor had a beloved daughter who, while playing on a swing, had injured her foot with bones protruding. No physician could heal her, so a reward was offered for anyone who could, consisting of two hundred gold coins and a mule. I went to answer the call, followed the methods in the book, and indeed she recovered. I then returned home with gold in my pouch riding the mule. My father was angry and suspected I had acquired too much wealth. At that time, bandits had already risen, and they assumed I must have engaged in unjust acts, seeking to report me to the authorities. My elder brother, who was a government scholar, plead my innocence, emerging from jail to question what had happened and subsequently produced the book. Upon learning that I had come out, my father rushed to my brother's house wielding a big stick. My brother repeatedly explained the situation but was not believed, and presented the book as evidence. My father grew even angrier and tore the book to pieces, throwing it into the fire. My brother managed to retrieve only four pages from the flames, and I hurriedly hid them and fled.[79p]
    What is currently used is merely a remnant of what once existed. Over the years, even the four pages have been lost. This is how it is recounted. Before long, I died from illness. Alas! Not even a remedy from the hands of the gods could save me. One is destined for nobility, while the other does not escape the hands of thieves; the only difference lies in what we utilize. Similarly, if an elderly immortal can embody the will of their forebears and not succumb to thieves, holding onto these skills to navigate the world, then figures like Lu and Bian and Hua Tuo would not have been so prominent. It is a pity that those who become engrossed in wealth and profit mistakenly assume the title of immortal, only to ultimately perish at the hands of thieves. Nevertheless, whether a person encounters an immortal or not depends solely on the depth of their fortune and virtue. The old immortal who receives their text yet fails to preserve their fortune is evident. I once saw in a record that a certain Hou Yuan, having met an old man who taught him military strategies, ultimately became a thief and brought about his own demise; the story bears strong resemblance to this situation. I often wonder why immortals do not find their rightful recipients. If they keep their teachings secret, why do they so often pass them to the wrong people, resulting in harm? How could an immortal bear such consequences? Furthermore, the path of Zhongnan is not necessarily that of true immortals. It is said that their ointment is made by refining the oil of young maidens’ bodies, filling the room with firelight, flames rising to the beams, and as the light fades, the ointment is formed. Is this truly a method for saving people? Traditional medicine involves the extensive use of insects and fish, leading to a delayed rise of ten years. Moreover, killing people to save others is not limited to one or two individuals, but potentially dozens or hundreds; such a so-called old immortal is merely a thief from beginning to end.[80p]
