As the sons arrived at the extended hall, gold and purple filled the entrance, obscured by bribes and favors, while the market grew rich, oppressing the children of commoners to serve as concubines; the crimes were too numerous to record, yet under the protection of the city and society, none dared speak out for thirty years.

    In the sixth month of the Xin Si year of Shaoxing, the Sichuan man Du Xinlao was appointed to the Southern Platform, planning to strike but had not yet launched an attack. At the same time, border conflicts arose; the former leader first arranged treasures to flee to the south, causing unrest in the capital. The emperor heard of this and was displeased. Liu Wuzhong, the general of Jingkou, proposed a preemptive strike, advocating for military action. The emperor's thoughts remained hesitant and indecisive, while he himself wished to respond with force. The former leader, naturally timid, still hoped for the firm peace to secure his safety, and thus he subtly remarked to the emperor: the border regions had no real issues; it was merely new officials eager to display their military prowess for credit. If they were to execute one or two people, peace could be restored.[28p]

    上不答，徐谓侍貂曰：是欲我斩刘锜耶？于是素轧其下而不得逞者，颇浸润及之矣。金亮索我大臣，廷遣徐嚞、张抡往聘。亮以非指，使谏议大夫韩汝嘉至盱眙止之，更令遣所索。奏至，上适在刘婕妤阁，当馈辍食，婕妤怪之，问诸侍貂而得其由，进说宽譬，颇与继先之言符。上大惊，问曰：汝安得此？婕妤不能隐，具以所闻对，遂益怒。丁未，诏婕妤归别第。莘老遂上疏列其十罪。初进读，玉色犹怫然。莘老扣榻曰：臣以执法事陛下，不能去一医，死不敢退。犹未许。[29p]

    The emperor did not respond, and Xu said to the attendant, "Does he want me to execute Liu Qi?" At that time, those who had been obstructed and unable to act were gradually influenced by it. Jin Liang demanded that our ministers send Xu Yi and Zhang Lun on an envoy mission. Liang used improper means and sent the advisor Han Rujia to stop them at Xuyi, and ordered them to send what was requested. When the memorial reached the emperor, he happened to be in the pavilion of Consort Liu, about to take a break from his meal. The consort was puzzled and inquired about it from the attendants, learning the reason. She proceeded to explain with broad examples, which somewhat corresponded with earlier discussions. The emperor was greatly shocked and asked, "How did you come to know this?" The consort could not conceal it and answered with all she had heard, which only intensified his anger. On Dingwei day, an edict was issued for the consort to return to her separate residence. Shen Lao subsequently submitted a memorial listing her ten offenses. Initially, when he read it, his face still showed displeasure. Shen Lao knocked on the platform and said, "As I enforce the law for Your Majesty, I cannot overlook even a physician; I would rather die than step back." He still did not grant permission.[29p]

    因密言外议，谓继先以左道幸，恐谤议丛起，臣且不忍听。上始变色首肯。罢朝，使宣旨曰：朕以显仁饵汝药，故假尔宠。今言者如此，当不复有面目见朕。期三日有施行，其自图之！辛亥，遂诏继先居于福，子孙勒停，都城田宅皆没官，奴婢之强，鬻者从便。令下，中外大悦。继先以先事闻诏，多藏远徙，故虽籍不害其富也。迄今其故居，华栋雕甍，犹号巨室。一传而子弟荡析，至不能家。或者谓其致不以道，宜于厚亡。赵性之作《中兴遗史》，载继先始末极详，参以所闻而着其事。
    　　《癸辛杂识》：王医师有二：王继先，高宗朝国医，后以德寿宫进药罔效，安置福州。王泾亦继先同时，相先后应奉，后以德寿疾，进凉药大渐，杖脊鲸海上，后得归。所谓御诊王承宣者是也。[30p]

    Due to secret discussions among outsiders regarding Ji Xian, claiming he had benefited from unorthodox methods, I feared that slander would arise, and I could not bear to listen. The Emperor began to change color and nodded in acknowledgment. He dismissed the court and ordered a proclamation stating: "I, with great benevolence, have granted you favor; thus, you have benefited. Such words as these will not allow you to see me again. Within three days, you shall have provisions for your own planning!" On the day of Xin Hai, he commanded Ji Xian to reside in Fuzhou, with his descendants' properties in the capital confiscated by the state; the strong servants and those sold off were allowed to be traded freely. After this order, there was great joy both inside and outside the court. Ji Xian had hidden many possessions and moved them far away, so even with confiscation, his wealth was not harmed. To this day, his former residence, adorned with magnificent beams and intricately carved eaves, is still called a grand estate. As his descendants spread apart, they could no longer sustain themselves. Some said his downfall came because he did not adhere to the proper way and thus was justly met with ruin. Zhao Xingzhi's "Records of the Restoration" meticulously details the beginnings and ends of Ji Xian, incorporating what he had heard about these events.

    In "Miscellaneous Records of Gui Xin": There were two physicians by the name Wang: Wang Jixian, the national physician during the reign of Emperor Gaozong, who later, for offering ineffective medicines at the De Shou Palace, was assigned to Fuzhou. Wang Jing was also of the same time as Ji Xian, serving in succession; later, while treating De Shou's illness, he prescribed cooling medicines but faced difficulties, ultimately returning home. The so-called imperial physician Wang Chengxuan refers to him.[30p]

    　　《金史》程辉传：辉拜参知政事，性喜杂学，尤好论医。从河间刘守真说，率用凉药。神童尝添寿者方数岁，辉召之，因书医非细事四字。添寿涂细字，改书作相。辉颇惭。人亦以此为中其病云。
    　　《剪胜野闻》：徐魏国公达病疽疾笃，帝数往视之。大集医徒，治疗且久，病少差。帝忽赐膳，魏公对使者流涕而食之，密令医人逃去。未几告薨，亟报帝。帝蓬跣担纸钱，道哭至第，命收斩医徒。夫人大哭，出拜帝，帝慰之曰：嫂勿为后虑，有朕存焉。因为周其后事而去。
    　　《金台纪闻》：金华戴原礼，国初名医。常被召至南京，见一医家，迎求溢户，酬应不闲，原礼意必深于术者，因注目焉。按方发剂，皆无他异，退而怪之，日往观焉。偶一人求药者，既去，追而告之曰：临煎时，下锡一块。麾之去。原礼始大异之。念无以锡人煎剂法，叩之，答曰：是古方耳。原礼求得其书，乃饧字耳。原礼急为正之。呜呼！不辨饧锡而医者，世胡可以弗谨哉？[31p]

    In the "History of Jin," the biography of Cheng Hui: Hui was appointed as a minister and was fond of eclectic knowledge, especially in discussing medicine. From Liu Shou-zhen of Hejian, he often used cool medicines. A prodigy who had added years to his life at a young age was summoned by Hui, who wrote the four characters "medicine is not trifling." The prodigy mistyped a character, changing it to "相" (xiāng). Hui felt quite ashamed, and people said he had hit upon his illness.

    In "Records from the Wild," it is said: Duke Wei of Xu suffered from severe boils and the emperor visited him several times. A large gathering of doctors treated him for a long time but his condition showed little improvement. The emperor suddenly granted him a meal, and the duke wept as he ate it, secretly instructing the physicians to flee. Not long after, he passed away, and the news was quickly reported to the emperor. The emperor, in disarray, mourned and rushed to the duke's residence, commanding the execution of the physicians. The duke’s wife wept greatly, and upon coming out to pay her respects to the emperor, he comforted her, saying: "Sister, do not worry for the future; I will take care of it." He then made arrangements for the aftermath and departed.

    In "Records of the Golden Terrace": Dai Yuanli of Jinhua was a renowned doctor at the beginning of the Jin dynasty. He was often summoned to Nanjing, where he visited a family of physicians. They welcomed him at their door and were quite busy responding to inquiries. Yuanli felt that their understanding must be profound, so he observed them closely. The prescriptions and medicines they prepared were all ordinary; he found this puzzling and visited them repeatedly. One day, someone sought medicine, and after leaving, Yuanli chased after him and advised: "When preparing, add a piece of tin." The man waved him off. Yuanli was greatly surprised. Reflecting that no one adds tin when preparing medicine, he asked and was told: "This is an ancient recipe." Yuanli found the source of this knowledge and it turned out to be just the character for syrup. He urgently corrected it. Alas! How can physicians, who cannot distinguish between syrup and tin, not be cautious in their practice?[31p]

    　　《长安客话》：太医院例，于端阳日差官至南海子捕虾蟆挤酥以合药，制紫金锭。某张大其事，备鼓吹旗旛，喧阗以往。有嘲以诗曰：抖擞威风出凤城，喧喧鼓吹拥霓旌。入林披莽如虓虎，捉得虾蟆剜眼睛。
    　　民间妇有精通方脉者，由各衙门选取，以至司礼监御医。会选中者，著名籍以侍诏，妇女多荣之，名曰医婆。
    　　《尘余》：有名医将入蜀，见负薪者，猛汗于河中浴。医曰：此人必死，随而救之。其人入店中，取大蒜细切，热面浇之，食之，汗出如雨。医曰：贫下人且知药，况富贵乎？遂不入蜀。
    　　《霏雪录》：木鳖不可服。苏门一人生二子，皆切爱之，恣其食啖，遂成痞疾。其父得一方，以木鳖煮猪肉食之。其次者当夜死，明日长者死。愚人不谨，轻信妄为，至杀其二子，悲哉！友人马君文诚得方书一帙，亦载此方，故为注其事于左以为戒。此仁之一端也。[32p]

    "Talks of Chang'an": It was customary in the Imperial Medical Academy that on the Dragon Boat Festival, officials would go to Nanhai to catch toads, prepare oil from them, and make purple gold ingots. One person made a grand affair of it, preparing drums, flutes, and banners, making a great din on the way. Someone mocked this in a poem saying: "Shaking off authority, I leave Fengcheng, surrounded by drums and flags, the vibrant banners shining. Entering the forest like a fierce tiger, I catch a toad and poke out its eyes."

    In the common folk, there were women skilled in medicine and pulse diagnosis, chosen from various departments to serve under the Imperial Medical Bureau. Those selected were honored with royal appointments, and many women took pride in this, calling them "medicinal mothers."

    "In the Dust": A famous doctor was about to enter Shu when he saw a man carrying firewood, sweating profusely while bathing in the river. The doctor said: "This man will surely die," but hurried to save him. The man went into an inn, took garlic, minced it finely, poured hot water over it, ate it, and sweated profusely like rain. The doctor remarked: "Even the poor know of medicine; what about the rich and noble?" Thus, he did not enter Shu.

    "In the Fei Xue Record": It is not advisable to consume wood frogs. A man from the Su family had two sons, both deeply loved, indulging them in their eating. They developed a chronic affliction. The father received a prescription that involved cooking wood frogs with pork. The younger son died that night, and the elder died the next day. The fool was careless, easily believing in false remedies, leading to the deaths of his two sons; how tragic! A friend, Ma Junwen, truly obtained a collection of medical prescriptions, which also included this prescription, thus noting this incident as a caution on the left. This is one aspect of benevolence.[32p]

    　　一童子头有疡，遇人以药敷之，童子头痒不可忍，爬搔见血，至以头触柱，至夕竟死。盖其药有砒，见血即害人矣。吾闻之文诚云。
    　　《涧泉日记》：李生者，居余干门外，善货殖，日卖养脾丸于市，尝揭巨榜于前曰：不使丁香木香合，则天诛地灭。家畜二婢，以事炮制。李一旦饮醉，而溺死于河，其家弗知也，但惟连日弗归，遣亲信四方寻求，略无踪迹。洎官验视，或有报其家者，亟前诣之，已腐败，仅能辨认，欲求免洗涤，已不及矣。遂槁葬于藂冢间，立木牌于坟，云：贾药李郎中之墓。或有题于牌后曰：卖药李郎中，昂藏辨不穷。一朝天赐报，溺死运河东。未几，家计萧然，其妻遣去二婢，寻弃所居，携二子以事人。或有问于妻曰：尔夫修合不苟，天当佑之，何返报之酷耶？他日，后夫醉之以酒，扣之，妻云：向所遣去二婢，先夫专委之修合，一名曰木香，一名曰丁香，其实不用二药也。故受斯报云。[33p]

    A boy had a sore on his head, and when someone applied medicine to it, the boy's head became unbearably itchy. He scratched until it bled and eventually struck his head against a post. By evening, he died. The medicine contained arsenic, which harms a person when it comes into contact with blood. I heard this from Wen Cheng.

    In the "Diary of Mountain Springs": There was a man named Li, living outside the gate of Yugan. He was skilled in commerce and daily sold spleen-nourishing pills in the market. He once hung a large sign saying, "If the cloves and sandalwood are not mixed, heaven will strike and the earth will be destroyed." He had two servant girls to assist him in preparing the medicine. One day, Li got drunk and drowned in the river. His family was unaware, but he did not return home for several days, prompting them to send trusted friends to search for him, but there was no trace. When the officials investigated, some reported his family, who hurriedly went to the site and found his body in a state of decay, only able to identify it with great difficulty. They wished to wash it but it was too late. Thus, they buried him in a place with piles of grass and erected a wooden sign at the grave that said: "The Grave of Doctor Li, the Merchant of Medicine." Someone later wrote on the back of the sign: "Li, the Merchant of Medicine, robust and discerning in trade. One day, heaven granted him a report, and he drowned in the Eastern Canal." Soon after, the family's finances deteriorated. His wife sent away the two servant girls, abandoned their home, and took her two children to serve others. Someone asked her, "Your husband was not negligent in his work; surely heaven should favor him. Why such harsh retribution?" On another day, her later husband got her drunk and questioned her. She replied, "The two servant girls I sent away were entrusted to the care of my late husband, one named Sandalwood and the other named Clove, but in truth, neither medicine was used. Thus, he received this punishment."[33p]

    　　　杂录

    　　古谚语：有病不治，常得中医。

    　　《战国策》：张丑谓韩相国曰：人之所以善扁鹊者，为有拥肿也。使善扁鹊而无拥肿也，则人莫之为之也。今君以所事善平原君者，为恶于秦也；而善平原君，乃所以恶于秦也。愿公之熟计之也！
    　　《尸子》：有医竘者，秦之良医也。为宣王割痤，为惠王疗痔，皆愈。张子之背肿，命竘治之，谓医竘曰：背非吾背也，任之制焉。治之遂愈。竘诚善治疾也，张子委制焉。夫身为国亦犹此，必有所委制然后治。[34p]

    Miscellaneous Records

    Ancient proverb: If one is sick and does not seek treatment, one often recurs to traditional Chinese medicine.

    From "Strategies of the Warring States": Zhang Chou said to Han Xiangguo: The reason why people appreciate Bian Que is that he treats swellings. If there were no swellings, no one would seek him out. Now, you, sir, are in favor of Pingyuan Jun, which is seen as adverse by Qin; yet it is your support for Pingyuan Jun that makes you adverse to Qin. I urge you to consider this carefully!

    From "Shizi": There was a physician named Qu, who was a good doctor in Qin. He treated King Xuan for carbuncles and cured King Hui of hemorrhoids, both successfully. When Zhang Zi's back was swollen, he instructed Qu to treat it, saying: "This back is not my own, so let it be managed as it is." After treatment, it was cured. Indeed, Qu was skilled in treating illnesses, and Zhang Zi entrusted him with the management. The body of the nation is also like this; one must be entrusted with authority before treatment can begin.[34p]

    　　《韩子》：医善吮人疡，含人血，非有肌骨之亲也，利之所在也。
    　　《庄子》天地篇：有虞氏之药疡也，秃而施髢，病而求医，孝子操药以修慈父，其色燋然，圣人羞之。
    　　庚桑楚篇：南荣趎曰：里人有病，里人问之，病者能言其病，然其病，病者犹未病也。若趎之闻大道，譬犹饮药以加病也，趎愿闻卫生之经而已矣。老子曰：卫生之经，能抱一乎？能勿失乎？能无卜筮而知吉凶乎？能止乎？能已乎？能舍诸人而求诸己乎？能翛然乎？能侗然乎？能儿子乎？儿子终日嗥而嗌不嘎，和之至也：终日握而手不掜，共其德也；终日视而目不瞚偏，不在外也。行不知所之，居不知所为，与物委蛇而同其波，是卫生之经已。南荣趎曰：然则是至人之德已乎？曰：非也。是乃所谓冰解冻释。夫至人者，相与交食乎地而交药乎天，不以人物利害相撄，不相与为怪，不相与为谋，不相与为事，翛然而往，侗然而来，是谓卫生之经已。[35p]

    "Han Zi": A good physician can suck out a person's wound, drawing human blood. This is not out of kinship in flesh and bone, but because of its beneficial nature.

    "Zhuangzi - Heaven and Earth": In the medicine of the Yu clan, there are ailments that are bald and afflicted. When ill, one seeks a doctor. A filial son administers medicine to care for his kind father, his complexion burning with color; the sage is ashamed of this.

    "Geng Sang Chu": Nan Rong Zhu said: When someone in the village is ill, the villagers ask about it. The sick can describe their ailments, yet even so, they may not truly be sick. If Zhu understands the great way, it's like drinking poison to treat an illness. Zhu wishes only to understand the classics of health. Laozi said: In the classics of health, can one embrace unity? Can one avoid loss? Can one know fortune and misfortune without divination? Can one be still? Can one cease? Can one set aside others and seek oneself? Can one be carefree? Can one be untroubled? Can one be like a child? A child howls all day without restraint; this is the utmost harmony: to hold on all day without twitching fingers, sharing its virtue; to gaze all day without squinting eyes, not looking outward. Acting without knowing where to go, residing without knowing what to do, flowing with things and merging with their rhythm—this is the classic of health. Nan Rong Zhu said: So this is the virtue of the ultimate person? It was replied: No. This is what is known as ice melting and thawing. The ultimate person engages harmoniously with the earth and communes with the heavens, not clashing over gain or loss, not finding strangeness in each other, not conspiring with one another, not engaging in matters with one another, coming and going carefreely—that is called the classic of health."[35p]

    　　《墨子》贵义篇：子墨子曰：惟其可行，譬若药然，草之本，天子食之以顺其疾，岂曰一草之本而不食哉？今农夫入其税于大人，大人为酒醴粢盛，以祭上帝鬼神，岂曰贱人之所为而不享哉？故虽贱人也，上比之农，下比之药，曾不若一草之本乎？且主君亦尝闻汤之说乎？昔者，汤将往见伊尹，令彭氏之子御。彭氏之子半道而问曰：君将何之？汤曰：将往见伊尹。彭氏之子曰：伊尹，天下之贱人也。君若欲见之，亦令召问焉，彼受赐矣。汤曰：非女所知也。今有药于此，食之则耳加聪，目加明，则吾必说而强食之。今夫伊尹之于吾国也，譬之良医善药也，而子不欲吾见伊尹，是子不欲吾善也。因下彭氏之子，不使御。[36p]

    In the "Essence of Mozi," Mozi said: "It is only that which can be practiced; it is like medicine. The root of a plant, the Son of Heaven consumes it to alleviate his illness; how can one say it is just the root of a grass and not consume it? Now, when a farmer presents his taxes to a great lord, the lord offers wine and grains as offerings to the deities and spirits; how can one say it is only something done by common folk and not partake? Therefore, even though they are of low status, in comparison to the farmer above, and the medicine below, can it be said they are not as good as the root of a grass? Moreover, has the sovereign also heard the tale of Tang? In ancient times, Tang was to visit Yi Yin and had the son of the Peng family serve as his charioteer. Halfway there, the son asked, 'Where is the lord going?' Tang said, 'I am going to see Yi Yin.' The son of the Peng family said, 'Yi Yin is among the lowly in the world. If you wish to see him, you should rather summon and question him, as he has received rewards.' Tang responded: 'This is not something you understand. Now, if there is a medicine here that enhances hearing and vision, I will surely advocate for it and force myself to consume it. As for Yi Yin in our state, he is like a good doctor or good medicine; if you do not wish for me to see Yi Yin, then you do not wish for my well-being.' Thus, Tang sent the son of the Peng family away and did not allow him to serve."[36p]

    　　《列子》周穆王篇：秦人逢氏有子少而惠，及壮而有迷罔之疾，闻歌以为哭，视白以为黑，飨香以为朽，尝甘以为苦，行非以为是。意之所之，天地四方，水火寒暑，无不倒错者焉。杨氏告其父曰：鲁之君子多术艺，将能已乎？汝奚不访焉？其父之鲁过陈，遇老聃，因告其子之证。老聃曰：汝庸知汝子之迷乎？今天下之人，皆惑于是非，昏于利害，同疾者多，固莫有觉者。且一身之迷，不足倾一家；一家之迷，不足倾一乡；一乡之迷，不足倾一国；一国之迷，不足倾天下。天下尽迷，孰倾之哉！向使天下之人，其心尽如汝子，汝则反迷矣。哀乐声色，臭味是非，孰能正之？且吾之言未必非迷，况鲁之君子，迷之邮者，焉能解人之迷哉？荣汝之粮，不若遄归也。[37p]

    In the "Zhou Mu Wang" chapter of "Liezi": The Qin people had a son named Shao, who was kind in his youth but became afflicted with a delusional disorder in his adulthood. He heard songs and thought they were wails; he looked at white and thought it was black; he savored fragrant food and thought it was rotten; he tasted sweetness and thought it was bitter; he mistook the wrong for the right. Wherever he directed his mind, the world was turned upside down—the four directions, water and fire, cold and heat—nothing was not distorted.

    Yang said to his father, "The gentlemen of Lu are skilled in various arts; will they be able to help him? Why don't you seek their counsel?" His father, traveling through Lu to Chen, encountered Laozi and mentioned his son's condition. Laozi said, "Do you not know the extent of your son's delusion? People today are all confused about right and wrong, muddled by profit and loss; many share the same affliction, yet none are aware of it. Moreover, one person's delusion is not enough to undermine a whole family; a family's delusion is not enough to turn an entire village; a village’s delusion is insufficient to topple a nation; a nation's delusion cannot bring down the world. If the whole world were to be as deluded as your son, you would be the one deluded instead.

    Who can correct the sounds of joy and sorrow, the colors and scents that define right and wrong? Additionally, my words may not necessarily be free from delusion; how can the gentlemen of Lu, who are themselves deluded, help to resolve the delusions of others? The grains of your honor are no better than a swift return home."[37p]

    　　《韩诗外传》：传曰：太平之时，无喑(疒龙)跛眇尩蹇侏儒折短，父不哭子，兄不哭弟，道无襁负之遗育，然各以其序终者，贤医之用也，故安止平正。除疾之道无他焉，用贤而已矣。诗曰：有瞽有瞽，在周之庭。纣之余民也。人主之疾，十有二发，非有贤医，莫能治也。何谓十二发？痿蹶逆胀，满支膈盲，烦喘痹风，此之曰十二发。贤医治之何？曰：省事轻刑则痿不作，无使小民饥寒则蹶不作，无令财货上流则逆不作，无令仓廪积腐则胀不作，无使府库充实则满不作，无使群臣纵恣则支不作，无使下情不上通则膈不作，上材恤下则盲不作，法令奉行则烦不作，无使下怨则喘不作，无使贤伏匿则痹不作，无使百姓歌吟诽谤则风不作。夫重臣群下者，人主之心腹肢体也。心腹肢体无疾，则人主无疾矣。故非有贤医，莫能治也。人皆有此十二疾而不用贤医，则国非其国矣。诗曰：多将熇熇，不可救药。终亦必亡而已矣。故贤医用则众庶无疾，况人主乎！[38p]

    "The Transmission of the Han Poems" states: In times of peace, there are no mute, lame, blind, hunchbacked, or deformed individuals; fathers do not weep for their sons, and brothers do not weep for their siblings. The way does not maintain the burdens of past care, yet each meets their end in sequence; this is the skill of a wise physician, hence harmony and stability. The method of dispelling ailments lies in no other but in employing the wise.

    The poem says: There are the blind, there are the blind, in the courtyard of Zhou. They are the remnants of Zhou's rule. The ruler's ailments number twelve; without a wise physician, no one can cure them. What are these twelve ailments? They are atrophy, stumbling, reversal, swelling, fullness, obstruction, blindness, agitation, breathlessness, paralysis, and turmoil. How does a wise physician treat them? By reducing burdens and lightening punishments, atrophy does not arise; by preventing the common people from suffering hunger and cold, stumbling does not occur; by preventing wealth from being hoarded, reversal does not happen; by ensuring granaries do not rot, swelling does not develop; by keeping treasury and storage full, fullness does not ensue; by not allowing ministers to indulge, obstruction does not manifest; by ensuring lower sentiments reach the upper echelons, blindness does not occur; by showing care for the lower classes, agitation does not transpire; by implementing laws diligently, breathlessness does not arise; by preventing resentment from below, turmoil does not manifest; by not allowing the wise to hide away, paralysis does not occur; and by preventing the people from singing and defaming, turmoil does not arise.

    The high officials and ministers are the core members of the ruler's body. If these core members are free from ailments, then the ruler is also free from ailments. Thus, without a wise physician, no one can heal. If everyone suffers from these twelve ailments and does not employ the wise, then the nation is no longer its own. The poem says: Many will be deeply entrenched and beyond remedy; ultimately, they will perish. Therefore, if the wise physician is employed, the common people will be free from ailments; how much more so the ruler![38p]

    　　《李氏春秋》：鲁有公孙绰者，告人曰：我能治偏枯。今吾俗为偏枯，药之则可以起死人矣。[39p]

    In "The Chronicles of the Li Family," it is stated: In Lu there was a man named Gongsun Chao, who told people: "I can treat deficiencies. Nowadays our customs are deficient, and by using medicine, we can bring the dead back to life."[39p]

    　　《孔丛子》：宰我使齐，反见夫子曰：梁丘据遇虺毒，三旬而后瘳。朝于君，君大夫众宾而庆焉。弟子与在宾列，大夫众宾，并复献攻疗之方。弟子谓之曰：夫所以献方，将为病也。今梁丘子已瘳，而诸夫子乃复献方，意欲梁丘大夫复有虺害，当用之乎？众坐默然无辞。弟子此言何如？孔子曰：女说非也。夫三折股而后为良医。梁丘子遇虺毒而获瘳，虑有与同疾者，必问所以已之方焉。众人为此之故，各言其方，欲售之以已人疾也。凡言其方者，称其良也，且以参据所以已之之方之优劣也。
    　　《新语》：昔扁鹊居宋，得罪于宋君，出亡之卫，卫人有病将死者，扁鹊至其家，欲为治之。病者之父，谓扁鹊曰：吾子病甚笃，将为迎良医治，非子所能治也。退而不用。乃使灵巫求福请命，对扁鹊而咒。病者卒死。灵巫不能治也。夫扁鹊，天下之良医，而不能与灵巫争，用者知与不知也。故事求远而失近，广藏而狭弃，斯之谓也！[40p]

    In the text from "Kong Congzi": Zai Wo was sent to Qi and later reported to the Master, saying: "Liang Qiu had been bitten by a snake and took three weeks to recover. When in the presence of the ruler, the nobles and guests celebrated. The disciples and the gathered guests offered cures. One disciple remarked: 'The reason for presenting remedies is because of illness. Now that Liang Qiu has recovered, why do the scholars again offer remedies? Do they suspect that Liang Qiu will encounter another snake bite and therefore wish to apply them?' Everyone sat in silence with no reply. What do you think of this disciple's statement?" Confucius replied: "Your assertion is incorrect. A good doctor often experiences three failures before succeeding. Given that Liang Qiu encountered a snake bite and recovered, he must think of those who share his ailment and will certainly inquire about the remedies. For this reason, everyone is discussing different remedies, wanting to sell their knowledge to those in need. Those who mention their remedies claim they are good as a way to compare the quality of their cures."

    In the text from "Xin Yu": In the past, Bian Que lived in Song, but fell into disfavor with the ruler, and fled to Wei. In Wei, there was a person gravely ill nearing death. When Bian Que arrived at their home to treat him, the patient's father said: "My son is critically ill and will have a renowned doctor come to treat him; you cannot help him." He sent Bian Que away. Instead, he summoned a spirit medium to seek blessings and incantations, cursing Bian Que. The patient eventually died, and the spirit medium could not heal him. Bian Que was renowned as the best doctor in the world but could not compete with the spirit medium. Those who sought distant help overlooked what was near, hoarding vast resources while neglecting what was right in front of them; this is what it means![40p]

    　　《淮南子》主术训：天下之物，莫凶于鸡毒。然而良医橐而藏之，有所用也。 【注：鸡毒，乌头也】
    　　缪称训：物莫无所不用。天雄、乌喙、药之凶毒也，良医以活人。
    　　良工渐乎矩凿之中，矩凿之中，固无物而不周。圣王以治民，造父以治马，医骆以治病，同材而各自取焉。【注：医骆，越医】
    　　说山训：病者寝疾，医之用针石，巫之用糈借，所救均也。
    　　为医之不能自治其病，病而不就药则悖。
    　　说林训：与死者同病，难为良医。

    　　忧父之疾者子，治之者医。

    　　人间训：患至而后忧之，是犹病者已倦而索良医也。虽有扁鹊俞跗之巧，犹不能生也。
    　　泰族训：所以贵扁鹊者，非贵其随病而调药，贵其擪息脉血，知病之所从生也。[41p]

    The "Huainanzi" on the main principles states: Among all things in the world, nothing is more toxic than chicken poison. However, a good doctor can extract and preserve it, finding use for it.

    Miao Cheng states: Nothing is unused. The poison of heaven's might, black beak, and medicine; a good doctor uses them to save lives.

    A skilled craftsman gradually perfects their work; within the realm of tools, there is nothing that does not conform. A sage king governs the people, a master craftsman governs horses, and a doctor governs illnesses—all use the same materials but seek their respective purposes.

    When a patient is bedridden, a doctor uses needles and stones, while a shaman uses incantations and offerings; the goal of salvation remains the same.

    If a doctor cannot manage the illness, and the patient refuses medicine, that is folly.

    In the forest teachings: It is difficult for a good doctor to treat someone with the same ailment as a deceased person.

    A child worries for a father’s illness; the healer is a doctor.

    In human teachings: To worry only after trouble arises is like a sick person who, exhausted, seeks a skilled doctor. Even with the talent of Bian Que or Yu Fu, one still cannot be revived.

    In the teachings of the Tai clan: The reason Bian Que is esteemed is not merely for adjusting medicine according to the illness but for feeling the pulse and blood, to understand the origins of the sickness.[41p]

    　　《史记》日者传：贾谊曰：吾闻古之圣人，不居朝廷，必在医卜之中。
    　　《释名》释姿容：脉摘，犹谲摘也。如医别人脉，知疾之意，见事者之称也。
    　　释疾病：疹，诊也。有结气，可得诊见也。
    　　《越绝书》：身死不为医，邦亡不为谋，还自遗灾。
    　　《盐铁论》：扁鹊不能肉白骨，微箕不能存亡国。
    　　扁鹊不能治不受针药之疾，贤圣不能正不食谏诤之君。
    　　所贵良医者，贵其审消息而退邪气也，非贵其下针石而钻肌肤也。
    　　吏不以多断为良，医不以多刺为工。
    　　扁鹊抚息脉而知疾所由生，阳气盛则损乏而调阴，寒气盛则损乏而调阳，是以气脉调和，而邪气无所留矣。夫拙医不知脉理之腠，血气之分，妄刺而无益于疾，伤肌肤而已矣。今欲损有余，补不足，富者益富，贫者益贫矣。严法任刑，欲以禁暴止奸，而奸犹不止，意者非扁鹊之用针石，故众人未得其职也。[42p]

    In the "Records of the Grand Historian," the biography of the sun states: Jia Yi said, "I have heard that the ancient sages, who did not reside in the court, would surely be found among the physicians and diviners."

    In the "Explanation of Names," it elaborates on appearances: "Pulse diagnosis" is akin to skilled deception. When a physician feels another's pulse, they understand the nature of the illness and make a corresponding diagnosis.

    Explanation of diseases: "Rash" signifies diagnosis. If there is a knot in the energy, it can be diagnosed and discerned.

    In the "Book of Yue," it states: "If one dies without seeking a physician, or a nation falls without strategizing, they are merely inviting disaster upon themselves."

    In the "Discourses on Salt and Iron," it is said: "Bian Que cannot restore flesh and bones; neither can a minor official maintain a fallen state."

    Bian Que cannot treat diseases that reject acupuncture and medication; wise and virtuous individuals cannot correct a ruler who ignores admonitions and criticisms.

    What is valued in a good physician is their ability to discern information and expel evil energies, not merely their skill in inserting needles and piercing the skin.

    Officials are not considered good based on making many judgments; physicians are not deemed skilled based on making many incisions.

    Bian Que knows the origins of illnesses by feeling the pulse — when yang energy is strong, it depletes and harmonizes with yin; when cold energy is strong, it depletes and harmonizes with yang. Thus, the energies balance, and evil energies find no place to reside.

    However, an inept physician does not grasp the principles of pulse intricacies and the divisions of blood and energy, recklessly stabbing without benefit to the illness, causing only harm to the skin.

    Now, to reduce the excess and supplement the deficient, the rich become richer, and the poor become poorer. Strict laws and harsh punishments aim to curb violence and stop wickedness, yet wickedness persists. The implication is that it is not Bian Que's needles and stones that are in play; thus, the people fail to fulfill their roles.[42p]

    　　《潜夫论》思贤篇：夫与死人同病者不可生也，与亡国同行者不可存也，岂虚言哉！何以知人且病也？以其不嗜食也。何以知国之将乱也？以其不嗜贤也。是故病家之厨，非无嘉肴也，乃其人弗之能食，故遂于死也。乱国之官，非无贤人也，其君弗之能任，故遂于亡也。夫生飰秔粱，旨酒甘醪，所以养生也。而病人恶之，以为不若菽麦糟糠饮清者，此其将死之候也。尊贤任能，信忠纳谏，所以为安也。而暗君恶之，以为不若奸佞阘茸谗谀者，此其将亡之征也。老子曰：夫唯病病，是以不病。《易》称：其亡其亡，系于苞桑。是故养寿之士，先病服药；养世之君，先乱任贤，是以身常安而国脉永也。上医医国，其次下医医疾。夫人治国如治身之象，疾者身之病，乱者国之病也。身之病，待医而愈；国之乱，待贤而治。[43p]

    In the "Thoughts on the Worthy" from the "Essays of the Hidden Scholar": Those who share the same plight as the dead cannot thrive, and those who walk alongside a fallen nation cannot endure. Is this merely empty talk? How can we know a person is unwell? It is because they do not crave food. How can we know a nation is about to fall into chaos? It is because they do not value the worthy. Therefore, in the household of a sick person, it is not that there are no fine dishes, but the person is unable to eat them, which leads to death. In the government of a chaotic nation, there are not no virtuous people, but the ruler is unable to employ them, resulting in its downfall.

    To live on fine rice and drink sweet wine is to nourish life. Yet a sick person loathes these, thinking them inferior to beans, wheat, and drinking clear broth; this is an omen of impending death. To respect the worthy and employ the capable, to trust the loyal and accept advice, is the foundation of security. Yet a blind ruler despises this, believing it is inferior to flattering sycophants; this is a sign of imminent destruction. Laozi said: "Only the ailing are ill, thus they do not suffer." The Book of Changes states: "Their loss, their loss, is tied to the mulberry." Therefore, those who cultivate longevity begin with treating illness through medicine; those who nurture a realm begin by restoring order through employing the worthy, thus they can keep themselves secure and their nations thriving.

    The highest physician treats the nation; the next, the illness. Governing a nation is akin to caring for one’s own body; sickness pertains to the body, chaos pertains to the state. The body’s ailments await a physician for recovery; a nation’s chaos awaits the worthy for restoration.[43p]

    治身有黄帝之术，治世有孔子之经。然病不愈而乱不治者，非针石之法误而五经之言诬也，乃用之者非其人。苟非其人，则规不圆而矩不方，绳不直而准不平，钻燧不得火，鼓石不下金，金马不可以追速，土舟不可以涉水也。凡此八者，天之张道，有形见物，苟非其人，犹尚无功，则又况乎怀道术以抚民氓，乘六龙以御天心者哉？夫治世不得真贤，譬犹治病不得良医也。治病当得人参，反得支罗服，当得麦门冬，反蒸横麦，已而不识真，合而服之，病以浸剧，不自知为人所欺也。乃反谓方不诚而药皆无益于病，因弃后药而弗敢饮，而惟求巫觋者，虽死可也。人君求贤下应，以鄙与真，不以枉已，不引真受猥官之，国以侵乱，不自知为下所欺也。乃反谓经不信而贤皆无益于救乱，因废真言，不复求进，更任俗吏，虽灭亡可也。三代以下，皆以支罗服蒸横麦合药，病日痁而遂死也。[44p]

    To govern the body, there is the method of the Yellow Emperor; to govern the world, there are the teachings of Confucius. However, when illnesses go untreated and chaos remains unaddressed, it is not due to errors in the acupuncture or medicinal practices, nor are the words of the Five Classics misleading; rather, it is because those who apply them are unworthy. If unworthy, then measures are not circular, standards are not square, ropes are not straight, and plumb lines are not level. Drilling will not produce fire, striking stone will not yield gold, a golden horse cannot be chased for speed, and an earthen boat cannot cross water. All these eight are aspects of Heaven's way, observable in form; if one is unworthy, still achieving nothing, how much less can one expect to wield the Way of governance and steer the hearts of the people, riding the six dragons? Governing the world without true worthies is akin to treating illness without good physicians. In treating illness, one must have ginseng, but instead receives pigweed; one must have ophiopogon, but instead steams common wheat. If one fails to recognize the genuine and mixes remedies, the illness exacerbates without realizing one has been deceived. Consequently, one mistakenly believes the prescriptions are insincere and that medicines are useless for the illness, thus discarding later remedies and daring not to ingest them, instead only seeking shamans—even if facing death, this is acceptable. Rulers should seek worthy individuals and respond with humility and truth, not to promote their own agenda, and should not draw true worthies into base positions. When the country is in turmoil, they do not realize they are deceived by the inferior. One thus mistakenly claims the classics are untrustworthy and that virtue is of no benefit for rescue, abandoning the true words and no longer seeking improvement, instead relying on common officials—even facing ruin, this is acceptable. Below the Three Dynasties, all used pigweed with steamed common wheat in their prescriptions, leading to increasing illness and eventual death.[44p]

    　　述赦篇：凡治病者，必先知脉之虚实，气之所结，然后为之方，故疾可愈而寿可长也。为国者，必先知民之所苦，祸之所起，然后设之以禁，故奸可塞，国可安矣。
    　　叙录篇：买药得雁，难以为医。

    　　《法言》重黎篇：扁鹊，卢人也，而医多卢。[45p]

    Treatise on Forgiveness: In treating illness, one must first know the strengths and weaknesses of the pulse and where the Qi is blocked; only then can a prescription be made, thus allowing for recovery and prolonged life. In governing a nation, one must first understand the sufferings of the people and the origins of calamities; only then can prohibitions be set, allowing for the resolution of deceit and the peace of the country.

    Record of Narrations: Buying medicine and obtaining a wild goose is a difficult task for a doctor.

    Words of the Law - Tribute to Li: Bian Que was from Lu, and many doctors were from Lu.[45p]

    　　《说苑》：三折肱而成良医。

    　　《申鉴》：药者疗也，所以治疾也，无疾则勿药可也。肉不胜食气，况于药乎？寒斯热，热则致滞阴。药之用也，惟适其宜则不为害。若已气平也，则必有伤。惟针火亦如之。故养性者不多服也，惟在乎节之而已矣。
    　　《外史》：士不辅而求霸，犹病者不用医而求愈也。
    　　张裘之行也，问临民之道于子。子告之曰：汝盍医乎？曰：何谓也？曰：世将无道，则天子有虚眩之疾，诸侯有怠荒之疾，百姓有饥馁之疾，大臣有贪蔽之疾，有司有夤缘侵渔之疾，受黜之臣有要君附权沽举之疾。夫国家如寄也，而有上下之险疾，扰而攻之，国焉不危？子欲临民，医是而已矣。曰：何以医也？曰：子能以仁义为药，以政教为汤，先瘳乎主疾，而后瘳乎诸侯臣庶，不亦善乎？曰：此夫子之医也，弟子何敢！逮曰：畏党将医诸侯，若诸侯瘳，国亦不危；诸侯不瘳，岂惟藩篱之祸，亦国之灾也。[46p]

    In "Shuo Yuan": Three折肱 can make a good doctor.

    In "Shen Jian": Medicine heals; it is used to treat illness. If there is no illness, there is no need for medicine. Just as meat cannot endure excessive food, how can it endure medicine? Cold leads to heat, and excessive heat can cause stagnation of yin. The use of medicine must be appropriate, otherwise it becomes harmful. If the vital energy is balanced, then there will surely be harm. The same applies to acupuncture and fire. Therefore, those who nurture their nature should not take too much medicine; it is only important to manage it in moderation.

    In "Wai Shi": A scholar who does not assist others while seeking power is like a sick person who does not rely on medicine while seeking recovery.

    When Zhang Qiu conducted his duties, he inquired about the way to govern the people from a wise man. The wise man advised him, saying: "Why not heal?" Zhang Qiu asked: "What do you mean?" The wise man replied: "When the world lacks proper governance, the emperor suffers from a false sense of grandeur, feudal lords become negligent, the common people face hunger, high officials fall prey to greed, and those in power engage in corruption. Those dismissed from office court favor from influential powers with harmful agendas. A state is like a home; when there are dangers both above and below, turmoil and attacks will arise, and the nation will be in peril. If you wish to govern the people, healing is all that is needed." Zhang Qiu asked: "How do I heal?" The wise man replied: "You can use benevolence and righteousness as medicine, and governance and education as broth. First, heal the illnesses of the rulers, then address the ailments of the feudal lords and commoners. Would that not be virtuous?" Zhang Qiu responded: "This is the art of healing from the master; how can the disciple dare to do this?" The wise man concluded: "Fear not, for by healing the feudal lords, if they recover, the nation will not be in danger. If the feudal lords do not recover, it is not only a calamity for the borders but also a disaster for the country."[46p]

    由此观之，士之交乎诸侯，犹医者之交乎疾，不能离也。而夫子云云，无欲以晋人之故而自释与？曰：子何以为士交于诸侯乎？昔郑人有膏肓之疾，三年而不出户。或告之曰：子之疾危矣，求良医以瘳之可乎？曰：市无良医，何以求为？曰：有良医者在于楚，子以百金致之，彼必不远千里而赴。郑人果得楚之良医以瘳其疾。是医者未尝求疾而治也，医在而疾者求之耳。然则为士者，亦诸侯之求之也，岂求诸侯而交欤？吾受晋王之聘，犹楚医受郑人之金也。郑人得之而瘳疾，晋王得之而不瘳，吾岂不若楚人之医乎？亦信与不信也。今党锢又告难于朝，王室之疾，殆甚于晋吾已矣哉！[47p]

    From this, we can see that the relations of scholars with feudal lords are akin to the relationships of physicians with their patients; they cannot be separated. Yet the Master asks, is it not out of desire for the Jin people that I release myself? One might ask: How can one consider the scholar's interaction with the lords?

    In ancient times, there was a man from Zheng who suffered from a serious illness for three years without leaving his house. Someone advised him, saying: "Your illness is grave; should you not seek a good doctor to cure you?" He replied: "The market has no good doctors; what can I seek?" The advisor said: "There is a good doctor in Chu; if you offer him a hundred pieces of gold, he will certainly travel a thousand miles to help you." The man from Zheng indeed obtained the good doctor from Chu and was cured.

    Thus, the physician does not seek out illness to treat; rather, it is the ailing who seek out the physician. Therefore, for the scholar, it is likewise the feudal lords that seek him out; he does not seek the lords for his connections. My acceptance of the Jin king’s invitation is like the Chu physician receiving the gold from the man of Zheng. When the man of Zheng received the physician, he was cured; if the Jin king does not achieve a cure from me, am I not similar to that physician from Chu? It is both a matter of trust and distrust. Now, the party strife has again made things difficult at court, and the illness of the royal family seems more severe than that of Jin already![47p]

    　　蜀王得征君而喜，闻有疾，忽焉而蹙，乃以单车造其门。征君知蜀王至，掩帷而卧，命李元候于阶。蜀王见李元而问曰：征君不能出欤？曰：不能。曰：寡人入以见可乎？曰：既卧矣，俟寤，然后请入。曰：征君得无癠乎？曰：癠不为疾也。曰：然则何为？曰：元闻之，天以薄蚀为疾，地以崩溢为疾。天子以幽厉为疾，王侯以骄泰为疾，士以贫蹇为疾，大夫以酷佞为疾，庶人以饥寒为疾。今征君之疾蹇也。国不举贤，使士有蹇疾，羇于道路，轩冕者多豺狼之群，膏粱者多犬豕之辈，由此观之，士安得而无疾也？夫疾以薄蚀崩溢，故天地以圣人为医；疾以幽厉骄泰，故天子王侯以贤士为医；疾以贫蹇，故士以梦卜为医；疾以酷佞，故大夫以明主为医；疾以饥寒，故庶人以循吏为医。然则征君之疾，药石有所不制，鬼神有所不虐，寒暑有所不侵，征于色而不见其槁，动于声而不见其戚，歌于鼓琴而不见其乐，叹于临川而不见其忧，此征君之所以为疾也。[48p]

    The king of Shu was delighted to see the ambassador, but upon hearing of his illness, he suddenly became distressed and went to his door with a single cart. The ambassador knew the king had arrived and closed the curtains to rest, commanding Li Yuanhou to wait at the steps. The king, upon seeing Li Yuan, inquired, "Is the ambassador unable to come out?" Li Yuan replied, "He is unable." The king asked, "May I enter to see him?" Li Yuan answered, "He has already fallen asleep, wait until he wakes, then you may ask to enter." The king then asked, "Is it possible that the ambassador suffers from a pestilence?" Li Yuan replied, "Pestilence is not an illness." The king said, "Then what is it?" Li Yuan explained, "It is known that heaven considers a subtle deficiency as illness, and the earth regards collapse and overflow as illness. The Son of Heaven sees hidden severity as illness, while kings and nobles perceive arrogance and extravagance as illness. Scholars consider poverty and difficulty as illness, and high officials regard cruel flattery as illness, while common people see hunger and cold as illness. Thus, the ambassador's illness is difficulty. The state does not promote the worthy, leaving scholars in distress, confined on the roads, with many chariots and official robes accompanied by packs of wolves, and the rich surrounded by dogs and swine. From this perspective, how can a scholar be free from illness? Indeed, illnesses arise from subtle deficiencies and collapses, thus heaven and earth employ sages as healers; illnesses from hidden severity and arrogance prompt the Son of Heaven and nobles to utilize worthy individuals as healers; illnesses of poverty and difficulty lead scholars to seek guidance through dreams; illnesses of cruel flattery urge high officials to seek wise rulers; and illnesses of hunger and cold cause common people to rely on diligent officials as healers. Hence, the ambassador's illness is one that medicine and stones cannot remedy, and ghosts and deities cannot torment; neither cold nor heat can invade it. It shows in his countenance yet remains unseen in his decay, resonates in his voice yet is not visible in his sorrow. He sings with the drum and lute yet does not express his joy, sighs by the riverbank yet does not reveal his worries—this constitutes the ambassador's illness."[48p]

    　　《中论》：怀疾者人不使为医，行秽者人不使画法，以无验也。
    　　《梁刘孝绰谢给药启》：一物之微，遂留停育。名医上药，爰自城府。虽巫咸视诊，岐伯下针，松子玉浆，卫卿云液，比妙众珍，宝云多愧。
    　　《颜氏家训》：医方之事，取妙极难，不劝汝曹以自命也。微解药性，小小和合，居家得以救急，亦为胜事，皇甫谧、殷仲堪则其人也。
    　　《中说》：内关之疾也，非有痛痒烦苛于身，情志慧然，不觉疾之已深也。然而期日既至，则血气暴竭，故内关之疾，疾之中夭，而扁鹊之所甚恶也。以卢医不能别，而遘之者不能攻也。
    　　《朝野佥载》：各医言虎中药箭，食清泥而解；野猪中药箭，啄荠苨而食。物犹知解毒之药，何况人乎？荠苨即沙参也，《尔雅》谓之菧苨。[49p]

    "Central Argument": A person suffering from illness should not be made a doctor, and one who is impure should not be made to follow the rules, as they lack validity.

    "Thank You for the Medicine" by Liu Xiaochuo of the Liang Dynasty: A very small matter can cause a delay in growth. Renowned doctors take medicine from the city. Even though shamans observe symptoms, and famous doctors administer acupuncture, treasures such as pine nuts and jade broths—that is to say, one might be embarrassed by the multitude of precious offerings.

    "The Family Instructions of Yan": In the matter of medicine, achieving subtlety is exceedingly difficult. I do not encourage you all to set your lives on it. By understanding the properties of medicine, even a small blend can be used in emergencies at home; this is also a worthy accomplishment, as were Huangfu Mi and Yin Zhongkan.

    "Central Discussion": The ailment of the inner gate does not manifest as pain, itch, or distress of the body; feelings and wisdom may lead one to be unaware of the depth of the illness. However, when the period arrives, the vital energy is suddenly exhausted, thus illnesses of the inner gate can lead to sudden death, which is what Bian Que detested the most. This is because Lu Yi could not distinguish it, and those who encounter it cannot attack it.

    "Miscellaneous Records of the Court and Countryside": Each doctor claims that a tiger struck by a medicinal arrow will eat pure mud to detoxify; a wild boar struck will peck at the jiachi (a plant). If animals know how to administer detoxifying medicine, how much more so humans? Jiachi is the same as sand ginseng, as stated in the "Erya" which calls it zhìni.[49p]

    　　《李氏刊误》：夫医切脉指下能知生死者，非天授其性，则因积学而致。然始或着能，末而寡效，论者以始能命通也，末缪数穷也。余曰：不然。其初屡中，喜于积财，记忆未衰，诊理方锐。及其久也，筋力已疲，志怠心劳，获效遂鲜。则始能末缪，于斯见矣。若以数之通塞，岂曰知理哉？
    　　《韩愈杂说》：善医者不视人之瘠肥，察其脉之病否而已矣。善计天下者，不视天下之安危，察其纪纲之理乱而已矣。天下者，人也。安危者，肥瘠也。纪纲者，脉也。脉不病，虽瘠不害。脉病而肥者死矣。通于此说者，其知所以为天下乎？夏殷周之衰也，诸侯作而战伐日行矣。传数十王而天下不倾者，纪纲存焉耳。秦之王天下也，无分势于诸侯，聚兵而焚之。传二世而天下倾者，纪纲乏焉耳。是故四肢虽无故，不足恃也，脉而已矣。四海虽无事，不足矜也，纪纲而已矣。忧其所可恃，惧其所可矜，善医善计者谓之天扶与之。《易》曰：视履考祥。善医善计者为之。[50p]

    "Li Shi Kan Wu": A physician who can discern life and death from reading pulses, if not endowed by heaven, has achieved this through accumulated learning. Initially, there may be skill, but ultimately, effectiveness diminishes. Scholars argue that in the beginning, skill is linked to the ability to command life, while in the end, it is related to the limitations of numbers. I say: not so. In the beginning, when one often succeeds, they delight in accumulating wealth; their memory is sharp, and their diagnostic skills are keen. As time passes, however, the muscles tire, willpower wanes, and the effectiveness becomes rare. The distinction between beginning skill and lacking later becomes evident here. If it is merely about numbers and their ebb and flow, can we truly say it reveals understanding of principles?

    "Han Yu Zaoshuo": A good physician does not concern himself with whether a person is thin or fat; he only observes whether their pulse indicates illness. A wise strategist does not regard the stability or danger of the world but observes the principles of order and chaos. The world consists of people. Stability and danger equate to fatness or thinness. The principles of order are the pulse. If the pulse is healthy, being thin is not harmful. If the pulse is ill but one is fat, they are doomed. Those who understand this perspective comprehend what it means to govern the world. During the decline of the Xia, Yin, and Zhou dynasties, the princes rose and waged war incessantly. For decades, the world did not collapse due to the continuity of order. When the Qin unified the world and did not allow any power to the princes, they amassed troops and set fire to them. The world collapsed in just two generations due to the lack of orderly principles. Therefore, even if the extremities are free of illness, they cannot be relied upon; it is solely about the pulse. Even if the world is at peace, it cannot be a source of pride; it is only about the principles of order. Concern for what can be relied upon and fear of what can be boasted of — this is what the good physician and wise strategist call heavenly support. The "I Ching" states: "Look at the foot and examine the auspicious signs." A good physician and wise strategist will do this.[50p]

    　　《松窻寤言》：人食五味以养五气，疾则扦味而气消，药以去之，凡使味之得畅也。人之道也，君臣父子以相生，桑麻谷粟以相养，有暴民生焉，侵弱犯尊，反厥民常，故刑以正之。甚者合党盛与，逆厥上罚，故兵以克之，暴者服而人道通矣。无疾则药不可试也，暴者泯则省刑戢兵可也。夫甘味肥膏，过食之且伤气，况于药乎。而况于舛施者乎。[51p]

    "On the Waking Words of Pine and Willow": People consume the five flavors to nourish the five vital energies; when afflicted, one alters the flavor and the energy diminishes, thus remedies are employed to restore balance to the flavors. This is the way of humanity, where rulers and subjects, parents and children, nurture one another; mulberry and hemp, grains and millet support one another. When there are violent people born among them, infringing upon the weak and offending the noble, it is necessary to correct this through punishment. In severe cases where factions grow strong and rebel against authority, military action is required to subdue them, allowing for order among the people. If there is no affliction, then medicine should not be tried; if the violent cease, then punishment can be reduced and military force can be restrained. Indeed, sweet flavors and rich fats, when overconsumed, harm one’s vital energy; how much more so with medicine? And how much more with those who apply wrong methods?[51p]

    　　《东谷所见》：方今药材，鄙贱者且数十倍于前，贵细者又数十倍于前，至携金繞市铺，求之不获者，人孰不知真药之难得如此。凡设铺而招人赎伪药者，愚也。赎伪药而愿疗病者，愚亦甚矣。吾辈宜何策？且宜于饮食衣服而加谨。古人首重食医，春多酸，夏多苦，秋多辛，冬多咸，调以滑甘，平居必节饮食，饭后行三十步，不用开药铺，饮食之加谨者此也。急脱急着，胜如服药。衣服之加谨者此也。或有疢疾，奉行不服药得中医之说，药石虽贵未害也。最是孝子慈孙，侍奉亲庭，岂忍坐视其病而不救？家有余蓄，尚可得良剂，贫窭所迫，将若之何？贫者固难得良剂，富者纵得良剂，又未必有良医。余用念及此，仰天而祝曰：愿天下人安乐！[52p]

    "What I Saw in the Eastern Valley": Nowadays, the cost of herbal medicine is several times more than before for the cheap and common varieties, and for the more precious and refined ones, it is also several times higher than before. Even when carrying gold through the market, one may search for it without success; who does not know how difficult it is to obtain genuine medicine? Anyone who sets up a shop to peddle fake medicine is foolish. And those who wish to cure their ailments by purchasing fake medicine are even more foolish. What then should we do? We should be more cautious about our food and clothing. In ancient times, people prioritized diet and healthcare: in spring they consumed more sour foods, in summer more bitter, in autumn more pungent, and in winter more salty, balancing with smooth and sweet flavors. They maintained moderation in their diet, walking thirty steps after meals, thereby avoiding the need for a pharmacy. Careful eating and dressing is essential. If someone were to suffer from a disease and insists on following the notion of not taking medicine from a doctor, the expensive herbal remedies would not harm them. Especially dutiful children and loving grandchildren who care for their parents at home—how could they bear to watch them suffer without providing help? If a family has resources, they can still find good remedies; but if one is poor, what can they do? The poor indeed find it hard to obtain quality remedies, and the rich, even if they find good ones, may not necessarily have good doctors. Reflecting on this, I look up to the heavens and wish: may all people in the world find peace and happiness![52p]

    　　《省心录》：欲去病则正本，本固则病可攻，药石可以效。欲齐家则正身，身端别家可理，号令可以行。固其本，端其身，非一朝一夕之事也。
    　　《艾子杂说》：艾子事齐王，一日朝而有忧色，宣王怪而问之。对曰：臣不幸，稚子属疾，欲谒告，念王无与图事者，虽朝然心实系焉。王曰：盍早言乎？寡人有良药，稚子顿服，其愈矣。遂索以赐。艾子拜受而归，饮其子，辰服而巳卒。他日，艾子甚忧戚，王问之故，蹙然曰：卿丧子可伤！赐黄金以助葬。艾子曰：殇子不足以受君赐，然臣将有所求。王曰：何求？曰：只求前日小儿得效方。
    　　《演繁露》：医有按摩法，按者以手捻捺病处也，摩者挼搓之也。字当从手，则其书当为按矣。《玉篇》手部无按字。《广韵》有按字，却从才别出，案字从木，注曰：几属也。[53p]

    "Record of Tranquility": To eliminate ailments, one must return to the root; when the foundation is solid, illness can be attacked, and medicine can be effective. To manage a family, one must correct oneself; when one’s self is upright, other families can be governed, and commands can be executed. Strengthening the root and uplifting oneself is not something that can be achieved overnight.

    "Miscellaneous Sayings of Aici": Aici served the King of Qi. One day, he appeared in court with a worried expression, and the king, puzzled, asked him about it. Aici replied: "I am unfortunate; my young child is ill, and I wish to report this, thinking that the king has no one to assist with these matters. Although I am in court, my heart is truly concerned." The king said: "Why not speak earlier? I have good medicine; if the child takes it, he will recover." He then instructed to provide it to Aici. Aici bowed in acceptance and returned, giving it to his son, who took it and died shortly after. On another day, Aici was deeply mournful, and the king asked the reason. He replied with sorrow: "Your servant has lost a child!" The king granted him gold to assist with the burial. Aici said: "A deceased child is not deserving of royal gifts, yet I have something to request." The king asked: "What do you seek?" Aici replied: "I only seek the method that was effective for my young child previously."

    "Expounding on Brocade and Dew": In medicine, there is a method of pressing and rubbing; the pressing involves using hands to knead the affected area, while rubbing involves rolling and massaging it. The character for 'press' should be derived from 'hand,' so its written form should represent pressing. In the "Jade Compendium," there is no character for 'press' under the hand section. In the "Broad Rhyme," the character for 'press' appears, but is derived from 'talent.' The character for ‘case’ is derived from 'wood,' noted as being related to 'table.'[53p]

    　　《栾城遗言箴》：眼医王彦若，在张文定公门下，坡公于文定坐上赠之诗，引喻证据，博辨详切高探，后学读之茫然。坡公敏于著述如此。
    　　《老学庵笔记》：陈亚诗云：陈亚今年新及第，满城人贺李衙推。李乃亚之舅，为医者也。今北人谓卜相之士为巡官。巡官，唐五代郡僚之名，或谓以其巡游卖术，故有此称。然北方人市医，皆称衙推，又不知何谓。
    　　石藏用，名用之，高医也。尝言今人禀赋怯薄，故按古方用药，多不能愈病。非独人也，金石草木之药，亦皆比古力弱，非倍用之，不能取效，故藏用以喜用热药得谤。群医至为谣言曰：藏用檐头三斗火。人或畏之，惟鼌以道大喜其说，每见亲友蓄丹，无多寡尽取食之，或不待告主人。主人惊骇，急告以不宜多服，以道大笑不顾，然亦不为害。此盖禀赋之偏，他人不可效也。晚乃以盛冬伏石上书丹，为石冷所逼，得阴毒伤寒而死。[54p]

    "The Deathbed Instructions of the Luan City": Wang Yanruo, an eye doctor, served under Zhang Wending. When Master Po presented him with a poem during a gathering at Wending's, he used metaphors as evidence, distinguishing himself with persuasive arguments and profound insights, leaving later scholars perplexed. Master Po was indeed keen on writing in this manner.

    "Notes from the Old Study Hut": Chen Ya wrote: Chen Ya has just passed the examinations this year, and the whole town is celebrating with Li Yatuo. Li is Ya's uncle and a doctor. In the North, those who forecast fortunes are called巡官 (Xunguan). Xunguan was a title for county officials during the Tang and Five Dynasties, possibly named because of their practice of traveling to sell their skills. However, people in the North refer to all doctors as Yatuo, without understanding the term.

    Shizang Yong, known for his medical expertise, once stated that contemporary people possess weak capabilities, thus relying on ancient prescriptions, many fail to cure diseases. It is not just humans; even medicinal stones and plants are weaker than ancient ones. Without doubling the dosage, effectiveness cannot be achieved, hence Shizang's preference for hot medicinals earned him slander. Other doctors spread rumors, claiming, "Shizang uses three buckets of fire from the eaves." Some feared him, while only Zhang revered his words. Whenever he saw friends storing medicinal elixirs, he would consume them without regard to quantity, sometimes without informing the host. The host would be alarmed and hastily warn against excessive intake, while Zhang laughed dismissively, yet it did not cause harm. This was due to a discrepancy in disposition, others could not replicate it. Ultimately, in the late winter, while lying on a stone, he ingested elixirs, and overwhelmed by the cold, succumbed to a hidden toxicity and died.[54p]

    　　《泊宅编》：蜀人石藏用，以医术游都城，其名甚着。余杭人陈承亦以医显。然石好用暖药，陈好用凉药。古之良医，必量人之虚实，察病之阴阳，而后投之汤剂，或补或泻，各随其证。二子乃执偏见，一概于冷暖，而皆有称于一时，何也？俗语云：藏用檐头三斗火，陈承箧里一盘冰。服金石药者，潜假药力以济其欲，然多讳而不肯言，一日疾作，虽欲讳不可得也。吴兴吴景渊刑部服硫黄，人罕有知者，其后二十年，子橐为华亭市易官，发背而卒，乃知流毒传气，尚及其子，可不戒哉！
    　　《墐户录》：许引寄医，常云：病与药值，惟用一物攻之，气纯而愈速。今之人不善为脉，以情度病，多其物以幸有功，譬之猎不知兔，广络原野，冀一人获之，术亦疏矣。一药偶得他味相制，勿能专力，此难愈之验也。[55p]

    "In the 'Bo Zhai Bian': A man from Shu, Shi Cang, made a name for himself in the capital with his medical skills. Another from Youyang, Chen Cheng, also became notable in medicine. However, Shi favored warming medicines while Chen preferred cooling ones. The good doctors of old would measure the strengths and weaknesses of a patient, observe the yin and yang of the illness, and then prescribe remedies, either to tonify or drain according to the condition. Yet these two hold their own biases, categorizing everything into hot and cold, and both gained renown in their time. Why? There is a saying: Shi Cang uses three dou of fire from the eaves, while Chen Cheng relies on a plate of ice in his chest. Those who take mineral medicines secretly use the power of the drugs to satisfy their desires, yet they often tend to avoid discussing it. When an illness suddenly arises, even if they wish to keep it hidden, they cannot. Wu Jingyuan from Wuxing took sulfur—few knew of it. Twenty years later, his son Tuo was made an official in Huating and died due to a back ailment, revealing the lingering toxicity that affected even his son. Should one not heed this?

    In the 'Jihu Lu': Xu Yinji, a physician, often said: The disease and the medicine must match; only one substance should be used to attack it for a quicker recovery. Nowadays, people are not good at feeling the pulse, but rather gauge illnesses with their emotions, often using excessive remedies hoping for efficacy. This is like a hunter who does not know rabbits, spreading nets across fields in the hope of catching one; the method becomes superficial. If one medicine coincidentally encounters another flavor that counteracts it, it cannot exert its full potency. This is a proof of difficulty in healing."[55p]

    　　《云麓漫抄》：医书论人脉有寸关尺三部，手掌后高骨下为寸，寸下为关，关下为尺。自高骨下至切寸脉指尽处，得寸为寸，则自切尺脉指尽处上至中指尖，岂非尺乎？古人以身为度，故寓于脉以言之。今医家但屈中指，以两纹尽处为寸，或侧手论夫长短，虽不相远，至问寸尺何以名脉，则不能答。
    　　《病榻寤言》：杨朱之友季梁有疾，其子三致医：其一矫氏之医曰：病在有生之后，欲攻其渐。季梁曰：众医也。其一俞氏之医曰：病在未生之前，其甚弗可已也。季梁曰：良医也。其一为卢氏之医曰：病出于禀生未形之先，齐生死而一之也。季梁曰：神医也。遣之而疾瘳。夫季梁之疾，三致医而疾瘳。余也齐居三月，内达于生死而疾自愈。若季梁则犹有外之心也。[56p]

    From "Yunlu Manchao": In medical texts, the human pulse is described as having three parts: the cun, guan, and chi. The area beneath the high bone at the back of the palm is the cun, below this is the guan, and below that is the chi. From the high bone down to the point where the cun pulse can be felt, that is the cun; thus above the point at which the chi pulse can be felt up to the tip of the middle finger, isn’t that the chi? The ancients used the body as a measure, hence they expressed it in terms of the pulse. However, modern doctors merely bend the middle finger, using the ends of two lines as the cun, or they measure length with the hand on the side. Although they are not far apart, when asked how to name the pulse as cun or chi, they cannot answer.

    From "Bingta Wuyan": Yang Zhu had a friend, Ji Liang, who fell ill. His son consulted three doctors: the first was a doctor of the Jiao family, who said: "The illness arises after birth; we should attack its gradual development." Ji Liang remarked: "Many doctors say the same." The second was a doctor of the Yu family, who said: "The illness arises before birth; it is extremely difficult to eradicate." Ji Liang commented: "This is a good doctor." The third was a doctor of the Lu family, who said: "The illness originates from constitution before it takes form, uniting life and death as one." Ji Liang said: "This is a divine doctor." After their consultations, his ailment healed. Ji Liang summoned three doctors and his illness was cured. I spent three months with him, reaching an understanding of life and death, and my ailment healed naturally. If I were Ji Liang, I would still have external concerns.[56p]

    　　《群碎录》：孔子主痈疽，赵岐以为痈疽之医。按《说苑》：雍睢，人姓名，赵岐传之误。
    　　《寒檠肤见》：昔者秦缓死，其长子得其术，而医之名齐于秦缓，其二三子者，不胜其忌，于是各为新奇而托之于父，以求胜其兄。非不爱其兄也，以为不有以异于兄，则不得以同于父。天下未有以决也，他日，其东邻之父，得缓枕中之书而出以证焉，然后长子之术，始穷于天下。少史子曰：有所讼者，必有所质也。苟不稽实，讼可听呼？是以争鸡之讼，有菽粟之证；争牛之讼，有放归之证；辨贼之讼，有摸钟之证。否则鼠牙雀角，穿屋穿墉者，谁能胜其辨耶？岂惟辨医缓三子之术而已哉？故曰：简不听。又曰：阅实其罪。[57p]

    "Records of Fragmented Thoughts": Confucius was an expert in abscesses, and Zhao Qi was considered a physician of abscesses. According to "Sayings and Essays": Yong Sui is a person's name, which was misattributed to Zhao Qi.

    "View from the Cold Eaves": In the past, when Qin Huan died, his eldest son inherited his techniques, and his medical reputation rivaled that of Qin Huan. However, his younger brothers, unable to surpass him in skill, each sought to create new and extraordinary practices attributed to their father in hopes of outshining their brother. It was not that they did not love their brother; they believed that without distinguishing themselves from him, they could not be valued like their father. There was no resolution in the world for this. Later, the father from the eastern neighboring state found writings in Huan's pillow and presented them as evidence, and thus the eldest son's techniques began to spread throughout the world. The historian's son said: In any dispute, there must be evidence. If one does not have solid proof, can the case be heard? Therefore, in disputes about chickens, there are proofs of beans and grains; in disputes about cattle, there are proofs of release; in disputes about thieves, there are proofs of touching the bell. Otherwise, against the evidence of mouse teeth and sparrow beaks, who can win their argument? Is it merely to distinguish the medical techniques of Huan's three sons? Hence it is said: Simplicity does not heed. It is also said: Examine the truth of their offenses.[57p]

    　　《比事摘录》：物有效吾所用而不能无弊者。病齿之人，服苦参，齿愈矣，而腰重不举。世不有类是者耶？蓝蛇有毒，尾却解毒；当归生血，须却破血；麻黄发汗，根节复止汗；酸枣醒睡，仁复令人贪睡；蝼蛄腰以前治大小便之过滑者，腰以后治闭结者，一物也，为用不同如此。又盐以浸鱼肉，则能经久不败，以沾布帛，则易朽烂，一物也，所施处各有所宜。惟医亦然。[58p]

    "Notes on Comparisons": There are things that are effective for our use yet cannot be without drawbacks. A person with bad teeth takes苦参 (bittersweet herb) and their teeth recover, but their waist becomes heavy and cannot lift. Are there not similar examples in the world? The blue snake is poisonous, but its tail has the antidote; Angelica revives the blood, yet it also breaks the blood; Ephedra induces sweating, yet the root can stop sweating; sour jujube awakens from sleep, yet its kernels lead one to long for sleep; the mole cricket treats issues of urination before the waist but causes constipation afterwards; all is one thing, yet the applications differ. The same applies to medicine.[58p]

    阿魏性极臭，用以入食饵，乃能去臭；葫气极荤，然置臭肉中能掩臭气，荤臭非所常宜也。君子医国，有兴废除害之责，人才用舍，可不审其所处者哉？　　《兼明书》：世上医人，见人病不能饮食，即云脾不磨者。明曰：按凫鹜鹅鸡之类，口无牙齿，不能噍嚼，须脾磨然后能消，故其脾皮悉皆坚厚。若人则异畜兽，既有牙齿能嚼食物，故脾皆虚软，惟气用化耳。病人脾胃气弱，即不能化食，非不磨也。家语云：龁吞者八窍而卵生，龃龉者九窍而胎生。胎卵既殊，脾胃亦别。而医人不喻斯理，一概而言，历代虽多，曾无悟者。
    　　《玉笑零音》：昔文公二竖入于膏肓，扁鹊识之；秦孝崔妃入灵府，许智藏识之。非察其疾也，乃诊其心也。
    　　《三余赘笔》：世言老医少卜，则医者以年老为贵，卜者以年少为贵。老医人皆知之，问之少卜，不知何谓？按王彦辅《尘史》云：老取其阅，少取其决。乃知俗语，其来久矣。[59p]

    Asafetida has an extremely foul smell, but when used in food, it can remove odors; the smell of rancid meat is strong, yet if placed among it, it can mask the foul odor, though the scent of raw meat is not usually appropriate. A gentleman who governs a country has the responsibility to promote what is beneficial and eliminate what is harmful; one must carefully consider the circumstances of人才 (talent) when employing or dismissing them, right?

    In the "Jianming Shu," it is said: In the world of medicine, when doctors see someone unable to eat or drink due to illness, they immediately say the spleen is not grinding. However, just note that waterfowl like ducks, geese, and chickens have no teeth in their mouths and cannot chew food; they need the spleen to grind it before they can digest. Thus, their spleens are all thick and hard. Humans, on the other hand, differ from other animals; they have teeth and can chew foods, so their spleens are soft and weak, primarily relying on the qi for transformation. When a patient has weak spleen and stomach qi, they cannot digest food, not because of lack of grinding. The "Jia Yu" states: for those that gnash and swallow, they have eight orifices and are born from eggs; for those that grind their teeth, they have nine orifices and are born from live birth. Eggs and live births are fundamentally different, and so are the spleens and stomachs. Yet, medical practitioners do not comprehend this principle and speak generally, despite many through history, none have gained insight into this matter.

    In the "Yu Xiao Ling Yin," it is recounted: In the past, Duke Wen's two servants entered into the vital organs, and Bian Que identified it; Empress Cui of the Qin entered the spiritual realm, and Xu Zhi Cang recognized it. This was not due to an examination of their ailments, but rather a diagnosis of their hearts.

    In "San Yu Zhuibi": It is said that old doctors are valued more than young diviners, as the former are esteemed for their experience while the latter are prized for their youth. Old doctors all understand this, yet when asked about the meaning, young diviners cannot explain it. According to Wang Yanfu's "Chen Shi," age brings experience, while youth brings decisive action. Thus, we know that this saying has existed for a long time.[59p]

    　　《长安客话》：太医院署有古铜人，虚中注水，关窍毕通，古色苍碧，莹然射目，相传海潮中出者。
    　　《清异录》：凡病膏肓之际，匕药难效，此针灸之所以用也。针长于宣壅滞，灸长于达气血。古人谓之延年火，又曰火轮三昧。
    　　《雪涛小说》：盖闻里中有病脚疮者，痛不可忍，谓家人曰：尔为我凿壁为穴。穴成，伸脚穴中，入邻家尺许。家人曰：此何意？答曰：凭他去邻家痛，无与我事。又有医者，称善外科。一裨将阵回，中流矢，深入膜内，延使治，乃持并州剪，剪去矢管，跪而请调。裨将曰：镞在膜内者，须急治。医曰：此内科事，不意并责我。噫！脚入邻家，然犹我之脚也；镞在膜内，然亦医者之事也。乃隔一壁辄思委脚，隔一膜辄欲分科，然则痛安能已？责安能诿乎？[60p]

    "Chang'an Guest Talk": In the Taiyuan Medical Office, there is a bronze figure, hollow and filled with water, with all its key points open. It has an ancient, verdant color, shimmering and dazzling, said to have emerged from the tide of the sea.

    "Records of the Unusual": When a disease has reached a critical point, bitter medicine is ineffective, hence the use of acupuncture and moxibustion. Needles are effective for promoting circulation and alleviating stagnation, while moxibustion is for enhancing the flow of qi and blood. The ancients referred to it as the fire of longevity, also called the three contemplations of fire.

    "Snow Wave Novels": It is said that in a certain area, there was a person with a painful foot ulcer, unbearable in its agony, who told his family: "Carve a hole for me in the wall." Once the hole was made, he extended his foot through it, reaching into the neighbor's house by a foot or so. The family asked: "What does this mean?" He replied: "Let it be so, I will endure the pain in the neighbor's house; it has nothing to do with me." There was also a physician skilled in surgery. A soldier was shot by an arrow, the shaft penetrating deeply into the membrane; he brought it to the physician, who took the scissors from Bingzhou and knelt to request treatment. The soldier said: "The arrowhead is embedded in the membrane and must be treated urgently." The physician replied: "This is an internal matter; I did not expect to be held responsible." Alas! Though the foot goes into the neighbor's house, it is still my foot; though the arrow is within the membrane, it is still the physician's matter. Yet one desires to partition a foot through a wall and separate disciplines between membranes; how then can pain cease? How can accountability be evaded?[60p]

    　　昔有医人自媒能治背驼，曰：如弓者，如虾者，如曲环者，延吾治，可朝治而夕如矢。一人信焉，而使治驼。乃索板二片，以一置地下，卧驼者其上，又以一压焉，而即躧焉。驼者随直亦复随死。其子欲鸣诸官，医人曰：我业治驼，但管人直，那管人死？呜呼！世之为令，但管钱粮完，不管百姓死，何以异于此医也哉。虽然，非仗明君躬节损之政，下宽恤之诏，即欲有司不为驼医可得耶？
    　　《遒徇篇》：用药治病，病好后便须抛药，犹复服药不已，必且积药成病。
    　　《求志编》：为政莫大于兵刑，民生莫重于医。是以周官有询听宥赦之详，无滥刑矣；田氏讲武之预，无败兵矣；十全十失之察，无庸医矣。今大理刑部都察审录重囚，用一己之见，都督都司挥户战敌，乏多算之谋，惠民药局废而不讲，奈何能兴亲民之治也。[61p]

    Once there was a physician who claimed to be able to treat hunchbacks, saying: "Like a bow, like a shrimp, like a curved ring, extend my treatment, and one can be cured in the morning and stand straighter by evening." A man believed him and sought treatment for his hunchback. The physician took two boards, placing one on the ground and having the hunchback lie on it, then placed the other board on top and pressed down. The hunchback straightened, but also died. The man's son wanted to report this to the authorities, but the physician said: "I aim to straighten hunchbacks; I don’t concern myself with whether people live or die." Alas! In the world today, those in power only care about taxes and resources, indifferent to the lives of the common people. How is this different from that physician? However, if not for the presence of a wise ruler who exercises restraint and implements benevolent decrees, could one expect the officials to be any better than a hunchback's physician?

    In the "Qiu Xun" chapter: When treating an illness with medicine, once the patient is well, they should stop taking medicine. If they continue, it will only accumulate and cause illness again.

    In the "Qiu Zhi" compilation: Nothing is more significant in governance than the military and punishment, and nothing is more important for the people's livelihood than medicine. Thus, the Zhou officials had detailed provisions for inquiries, pardons, and forgiveness, avoiding arbitrary punishment; Tan Shi discussed military preparedness, ensuring no defeat in battle; oversight of successes and failures, eliminating the need for ineffective physicians. Now, in the Supreme Court's criminal department, with individual perspectives, those supervising military operations lack strategic foresight, and the people's health programs are neglected. How can one expect to promote governance that truly cares for the people?[61p]

    　　　　　外编

    　　《芸窻私志》：神农时，白民进药兽。人有疾病则拊其兽，授之语。语如白民所传，不知何语。语已，兽辄如野外，(口衔)一草归，捣汁服之即愈。后黄帝命风后纪其何草起何疾，久之，如方悉验。古传黄帝尝百草，非也。故虞卿曰：黄帝师药兽而知医。
    　　《列子》汤问篇：鲁公扈、赵齐婴二人有疾，同请扁鹊求治。扁鹊治既，同愈，谓公扈、齐婴曰：汝曩之所疾自外而干腑脏者，因药石之所已，今有偕生之疾，与礼偕长，今为汝攻之何如？二人曰：愿先闻其验。扁鹊谓公扈曰：汝志强而气弱，故足于谋而寡于断；齐婴志弱而气强，故少于虑而伤于专。若换汝之心，则均于善矣。扁鹊遂饮二人毒酒，迷死三日，剖胸探心，易而置之，投以神药。既悟如初，二人辞归。于是公扈反齐婴之室而有其妻子，妻子弗识；齐婴亦反公扈之室而有其妻子，妻子亦弗识。二室因相与讼，求辨于扁鹊。扁鹊辨其所由，讼乃已。[62p]

    Outer Compendium

    "Private Thoughts on Herbalism": During the time of Shennong, common people sought medicinal beasts. When someone fell ill, they would caress the beast and convey words to it. The words were as transmitted by the common people, and their meaning was unknown. Once the words were spoken, the beast would immediately act like it was in the wild, picking a herb with its mouth, crushing it for juice, and taking it to recover. Later, the Yellow Emperor commanded Feng Hou to record which herbs arose for which diseases; after a long time, the formulas were fully verified. There is an ancient saying that the Yellow Emperor once tried all the herbs, which is not true. Therefore, Yu Qing said: the Yellow Emperor learned medicine from the beasts.

    "The Questions of Tang" in Liezi: Duke Hu of Lu and Qi Ying of Zhao both fell ill and sought treatment from Bian Que. After Bian Que treated them, they both recovered. He said to Duke Hu and Qi Ying: "Your previous ailments were caused by external disturbances affecting your internal organs, due to the effects of herbs and stones. Now there is a coexisting ailment that ages with the rites; what would happen if we attacked it together?" The two replied: "We wish to first hear your findings." Bian Que told Duke Hu: "Your will is strong, but your vitality is weak, which makes you full of plans but lacking in decisive actions; Qi Ying has a weak will but strong vitality, leading him to think little yet be harmed by fixation. If we exchanged your hearts, then both would attain balance in virtue." Bian Que then made the two drink poisoned wine, which caused them to be in a stupor for three days. He opened their chests and examined their hearts, switched them, and administered divine medicine. Once they awoke as before, the two departed for home. Duke Hu returned to Qi Ying's home and found his wife and children, who did not recognize him; Qi Ying likewise returned to Duke Hu's home where his wife and children also did not recognize him. The two households then had a dispute, seeking judgment from Bian Que. Bian Que discerned the cause of their confusion, and the dispute was resolved.[62p]

    　　《齐谐记》：钱塘徐秋夫善治病，宅在湖沟桥东，使闻空中呻吟声甚苦。秋夫起，至呻吟处问曰：汝是鬼耶？何为如此？饥寒须衣食耶？抱病须治疗耶？鬼曰：我是东阳人，姓斯名僧平，昔为乐游吏，患腰痛死，今在湖北虽为鬼，苦亦如生，为君善医，故来相告。秋夫曰：但汝无形，何由治？鬼曰：但缚茅作人，按穴针之讫，弃流水中可也。秋夫作茅人，为针腰目二处，并复薄祭，遣人送后河中。及暝，梦鬼曰：已差。并承惠食，感君厚意。秋夫宋元嘉为奉朝请。[63p]

    In "Records of Qi and Xie": Xu Qiufu from Qiantang was skilled in treating illnesses and lived east of the Hu Gou Bridge. One night, he heard pained moans from the air. Qiufu got up and went to the source of the sounds, asking, "Are you a ghost? Why do you suffer so? Are you hungry and cold and in need of food and clothing? Do you need treatment for your illness?" The ghost responded, "I am from Dongyang, my surname is Si and my name is Sengping. I used to be a fun-loving official but died from severe腰 pain. Though I am a ghost in Hubei now, my suffering is as great as when I was alive. I came to inform you because you are a good doctor." Qiufu said, "But since you are formless, how can I treat you?" The ghost replied, "Just bind some grass to form a human figure, apply acupuncture to my腰 at two points, and then cast it into the flowing water." Qiufu made a grass figure, applied needles at the two points on the腰, offered a thin sacrifice, and sent it into the river. When night fell, he dreamed of the ghost, who said, "I have been relieved. I also accept the food offered; thank you for your kindness." Qiufu then became an official during the Song Yuanjia period.[63p]

    　　《朝野佥载》：久视年中，襄川人杨元亮，年二十余，于虔州汶山观佣力，昼梦见天尊云：我堂舍破坏，汝为我修造，遣汝能医一切病。悟而说之，试疗无不愈者。赣县里正背有肿，大如拳，亮以刀割之，数日平复。疗病日获十千。造天尊堂成，疗病渐渐无效。
    　　如意年中，洛州人赵元景病，卒五日而苏，云：见一僧与一木，长尺余，教曰：人有病者，汝以此木拄之即愈。元景得见机上尺，乃是僧所与者，试将疗病，拄之立差。门庭每日数百人。御史马知己以其聚众，追禁左台，病者满于台门。则天闻之，追入内，官人病，拄之即愈。放出任救病百姓，数月以后，得钱七百余贯。后渐无验，遂绝。[64p]

    In the "Record of the Times" (朝野佥载): During the Jiushi years, a man named Yang Yuanliang from Xiangchuan, over twenty years old, observed at Wenshan in Qianzhu and dreamt one day of the Celestial Lord who said: "My hall is damaged; you will rebuild it for me and you are capable of healing all diseases." Upon awakening and understanding this, he tried healing, and there was no case he could not cure. In Ganyuan County, a local official had a swelling as large as a fist on his back; Yang used a knife to cut it, and after several days, it healed. Daily, he earned a thousand in money from healing. He completed the construction of the Celestial Lord’s hall, but gradually, the effectiveness dwindled.

    In the year of Ruyi, Zhao Yuanjing from Luozhou fell ill and, after five days, recovered, saying he saw a monk with a wood over a foot long, who taught him: “For those with illness, if you support yourself with this wood, you will be healed.” Yuanjing managed to find a similar length of wood and tried healing with it, which worked remarkably well. His door was crowded with hundreds of people daily. Inspector Ma Zhiji, noticing the gathering crowd, pursued a ban against this practice at the left platform, as patients filled the platform doors. Upon hearing this, Empress Wu summoned him into the inner court; there, government officials with ailments were healed by leaning on it. He was allowed to treat the people, and after several months, he earned over seven hundred strings of cash. Later, as efficacy declined, the practice eventually ceased.[64p]

    　　《稽神录》：陈寨，泉州晋江巫也。善禁祝之术，为人治疾多愈者。有漳州逆旅苏猛，其子病狂，人莫能疗，乃往请陈。陈至，苏氏子见之，戟手大骂，寨曰：此疾入心矣。乃立坛于堂中，戒人无得窃视。至夜，乃取苏氏子劈为两片，悬堂之东壁，其心悬北檐下。寨方在堂中作法，所悬之心，遂为犬食。寨求之不得，惊惧，乃持刀宛转于地，出门而去。主人弗知，谓其作法耳。食顷，乃持心而入，内于病者之腹，被发连叱，其腹遂合。苏氏子既悟，但连呼递铺递铺。家人莫之测。乃其日去家数里，有驿吏手持官文书，死于道傍。初南中驿路二十里置一递铺，驿吏持符牒以次传授，欲近前铺，辄连呼以警之。乃寨取驿吏心而活苏氏子，遂愈如故。[65p]

    "Records of the Divine Inquiry": Chen Zhai, a shaman from Jinjiang, Quanzhou, excels in the techniques of exorcism and has successfully treated many illnesses. There was a traveler from Zhangzhou named Su Meng, whose son was afflicted with madness that no one could heal. So, he went to seek Chen's help. When Chen arrived, Su's son saw him and shouted curses while pointing at him. Zhai observed, "This sickness has entered his heart." He set up an altar in the hall, warning everyone not to peek. At night, he took Su's son and split him in half, hanging the body on the east wall of the hall, with the heart suspended under the northern eaves. While Zhai performed his rituals in the hall, the heart was eaten by dogs. Zhai, unable to find it, became alarmed and, wielding a knife, turned around and left. The hosts were unaware and thought he was merely performing a ritual. After some time, he returned, holding the heart and entered the sick man's belly, his hair disheveled and shouting, causing the abdomen to come together. Su's son regained consciousness, repeatedly calling out "Pú pú." The family could not understand. A few days later, while traveling several miles from home, a postal official holding government documents died by the roadside. Originally, there were relay stations along the southern postal route every twenty miles, where postal officials passed on messages with talismans, and they would call out warnings to approach the next station. Zhai took the postal official's heart and revived Su's son, who fully recovered.[65p]

    　　《东坡杂记》：王斿元龙言：钱子飞有治大风方极验，常以施人。一日，梦人云：天使已以此病人，君违天怒，若施不已，君当得此病，药不能救。子飞惧，遂不施。仆以为天之所病不可疗耶？则药不应复有效。药有效者，则是天不能病。当是病之祟畏是药，假天以禁人耳。晋侯之病为二竖子，李子豫赤丸亦先见于梦，盖有或使之者。子飞不察，为鬼所胁。若余则不然，苟病者得愈，愿代受其苦。家有一方，以敷皮肤，能下腹中秽恶，在黄州试之，病良已，今当常以施人。[66p]

    In the "Records of Dongpo," Wang Fangyuanlong said: Qian Zifei had an effective remedy for severe wind disturbances and often administered it to others. One day, he dreamt that someone told him: "Heaven has decreed this illness for a man, and if you go against Heaven by continuing to treat, you shall suffer this illness yourself, and no medicine will be able to save you." Zifei, frightened, decided not to administer the remedy. I believe that the ailments decreed by Heaven cannot be treated, and therefore the medicine should no longer be effective. If the medicine is effective, it suggests that Heaven cannot inflict illness. This is an example of the affliction fearing the medicine, using Heaven as a means to prohibit human action. The sickness of Duke Jin was caused by two young boys, and Li Ziyu’s red pills were also revealed in dreams, indicating that there was some external influence. Zifei, not recognizing this, was coerced by spirits. However, if it were me, I would think differently; if a patient could recover, I would willingly bear their suffering. My family has a method that can be applied to the skin, which can cleanse the foulness from within the abdomen. It was tried in Huangzhou, and the illness healed well; now I intend to regularly administer it to others.[66p]

    　　《闻见近录》：张文定守蜀，重九药市，拂晨骤雨，随行医官张子阳避雨玉局观，须臾晴霁，树上白衣翁伫立，顾视子阳曰：我有一事，要尔通意主人。子阳唯唯。即出药二粒如粟米大，使遗文定。子阳曰：尝识尹否？翁姓何氏？翁曰：我姓葛，侍郎已两守蜀，我尝见之。子阳曰：止此一来耳。翁曰：说与主人，他日再来此相寻。子阳持药，具白文定。以汞一两，置药一粒煅之，须臾有声，如远磬然，清越非常，谛听间，忽有圆光出合内，焕耀满室，惊而取之，汞成黄金。文定乃饵其余药一粒。使再访之，不复见矣。[67p]

    In the "Recent Records of What Is Heard and Seen": Zhang Wendin was stationed in Shu. On the Double Ninth Festival, while attending the medicine market, he took shelter from a sudden morning rain beside Zhang Ziyang, an itinerant physician, at the Yujuguan. Soon, the skies cleared, and a white-clad old man stood among the trees. He turned to Ziyang and said, "I have something for you to communicate to your master." Ziyang nodded in agreement. The old man then handed out two medicinal pills, as small as millet, for Ziyang to deliver to Wendin. Ziyang asked, "Do you know Yin? What is your surname?" The old man replied, "My surname is Ge. I have served as a vice minister and have been stationed in Shu for two terms. I have seen him before." Ziyang noted, "This is your only visit, then." The old man said, "Tell your master that I will come again another day." Ziyang took the medicine and reported everything to Wendin. He took one grain of the mercury, combined it with one tael of the medicine, and soon it made a sound, like a distant bell, clear and extraordinary. Listening closely, a round light suddenly emerged from within, illuminating the entire room. Startled, he took it, and the mercury transformed into gold. Wendin then ingested the remaining pill, sending someone to inquire again, but he could not be found.[67p]

    　　《龙川别忘》：张安道知成都，日以医官自随，重九请出观药市。五更，市方合而雨作，入玉局观避之。至殿上，医见一道人临阶而坐，往就之，相问劳已。道人曰：张端明入蜀，今已再矣。医曰：始一至蜀耳。曰：子不知也。凡人元气十六两，渐老而耗，张公所耗过半矣。吾与之夙相好，今见子，非偶然也。解衣裙出药两圆，曰：一圆可补一两气。医曰：张公虽好道，然性重慎，恐未信也。道人曰：所以二圆，正为尔也。取一圆并水银一两纳铫中，以盏盖之，烧之良久，札札有声，揭盏以松脂木投之，觉有异，三投而药成，当知此非凡药也。医径归，白公试之如其言，每投松脂，焰起照所坐小亭，至三投，焰如金色，倾出则紫金也。乃服其一圆，而使医遍游成都，冀复遇焉。后见之孔明庙前，复得一圆药，然服之亦无他异。[68p]

    "Longchuan's Reminder": Zhang Andao, knowing about Chengdu, followed as a medical officer. On the Double Ninth Festival, he was invited to watch the medicinal market. At dawn, it began to rain as the market closed, so he entered the Jade Pavilion to take shelter. Upon reaching the palace, he saw a Daoist sitting at the steps. He approached and inquired about his well-being. The Daoist said: "Zhang Duanming has already been in Shu for the second time." The physician replied: "He has only just come to Shu."
